Provincial Grid Integration Project
CREDIT AND PROJECT SUMMARY
Borrower:           The Lao People's Democratic Republic (Lao PDR)
Beneficiary:        Electricite du Laos (EdL)
Amount:             SDR 25.3 million (US$36 million equivalent)
Terms:              Standard, with 40 years maturity
Onlending Terms:  The proceeds of the credit will be onlent by the Government to EdL,
under a Subsidiary Loan Agreement, with a 20 year term (including five
years of grace on repayment of principal) and an interest rate of 8%
per annum. EdL will bear the foreign exchange risk.
Project Objectives: The objectives of the proposed project are: (i) extending electricity
supply to more consumers in the Southern and Central Regions of the
Lao PDR; (ii) enhancing the quality of supply for existing consumers in
those provinces; (iii) developing additional power export prospects for
the Lao PDR; (iv) improving EdL's system efficiency; and
(v) continuing EdL's institutional development.
Project Description: The proposed project includes: (a) A provincial electrification compo-
nent, including construction of: (i) one 115 kV and three 22 kV over-
head river-crossing lines, to interconnect disaggregated local networks
with the Thai grid, and (ii) extension of the 22 kV networks in six prov-
inces of the country's central and southern regions. (b) A system effi-
ciency component, including: (i) action programs for EdL to reduce
system losses, improve billing and collections, and reduce arrears from
Government agencies, and (ii) equipment and materials to strengthen
EdL's operation and maintenance services, particularly at its provincial
subsidiaries. (c) An institutional development component that includes
equipment, material and services, and needed technical assistance for
upgrading the skills of EdL's staff and its training facilities. (d) A fu-
ture project development component that includes a feasibility study for
the installation of additional capacity at the Nam Ngum hydro station.
Benefits:           The main benefit of the proposed project would the extension of elec-
tric networks in dispersed areas in the country's central and southern
regions, thereby making already healthy economic activity in those
areas more cost effective. Additional benefits include (i) improving the
reliability of supplies to consumers throughout EdL's networks; (ii)
increasing the technical competence at all levels of EdL; and (iii) trans-
ferring to EdL new techniques for managing a dispersed enterprise.
This document has a restricted distribution and may be used by recipients only in the performance   I
of their official duties. Its contents may not otherwise be disclosed without World Bank authorization.

Risk:              The proposed project faces no major risks. Possible risks include minor
construction delays if: (a) EGAT does not complete its portion ot the
interconnecting links on time; (b) constraints persist in the supply of
cement; and (c) drought conditions so limit the availability of energy
for export and consequently EdLIs capacity to generate counterpart
funds. The proposed project includes measures to mitigate these risks.

1. THE ENERGY AND POWER SECTORS
Overview
1.1       The Lao PDR, a land loc :d country with an area of 236,800 km2 and a
population of 3.6 million, is one of the poorest in the world, with a per
capita Gross Domestic Product of US$210 per annum (1990). The largest city is
Vientiane, the capital, with a population of abouc 381,000. Some 40X of the
population lives in, and much of the country's economic activity centers
around, two narrow ribbons of development: (i) along the Mekong River and in
close proximity to the Thai border in the south, and (ii) in the Vientiane-
Luang Prabang corridor in the north. These two ribbons are clearly separated
from each other; elsewhere, the country is characterized by difficult terrain
(including rugged mountains and dense tropical jungle), sparse population, and
meager infrastructure.
1.2       The Lao PDR has identified deposits of coal, lignite, iron, copper,
lead, tin and other metals, many of which are unexploited. The country also
has vast forest reserves with about 130,000 km2 (some 58X of the land area)
under effective forest cover. About 90X of the energy consumed in the Lao PDR
is produced from fuelwood, with electricity providing 51, and petroleum prod-
ucts providing the remaining 51.
1.3       The Lao PDR has rich hydroelectric resources within its borders.
These resources have a potential of over 18,000 MW, of which only about 200 MW
have been so far been developed. Several studies of potential sites on dif-
ferent rivers are being conducted; however, because the country's sparse popu-
lation is widely scattered about a country characterized by rugged terrain and
deep forest cover, any hydroelectric development must be designed to export
electricity to Thailand over and above meeting modest domestic demand,/.
Some of the sites under study are very large, and their prospective develop-
ment as export projects offers the Government considerable potential to real-
ize foreign exchange earnings. However, the implementation of major export
projects requires (i) innovative institutional structures involving the Thai
Government (or agencies thereof), and third parties; and (ii) sophisticated
financial engineering. Hence, even the most attractive of these export pro-
jects involves a high degree of uncertainty. The Bank Group has recently
served as executing agency for a United Nations Development Programme (UNDP)
financed feasibility study of the Nam Theun II project, a highly promising
major export prospect.
1.4       In contrast to the abundance of fuelwood and hydroelectric poten-
tial, the Lao PDR imports all of its petroleum needs. Efforts to encourage
exploration for domestic hydrocarbons are in an early stage.
I/   Over the last ten years, electricity has been the Lao PDR's leading ex-
port. In 1990, electricity accounted for 281 of Lao export revenues.



- 2 -
B. The Power Sector
1.5       Electric power falls under the Ministry of Industry and Handicrafts
(MOI), which Is the parent for Electricite du Laos (EdL), the country's na-
tional utility. EdL has been responsible for (i) preparation, construction
and operation of all major generation and transmission facilities nationwide,
and (ii) distribucion of electricity in Vientiane and the Vientiane Plain.
Elsewh(re, electricity service had been provided by provincial utilities; on
June 30, 1991, EdL acquired tb- five utilities (para. 2.5) serving the prov-
inces receiving benefits from the IDA-financed Southern Provinces Electrifica-
tion Project (Credit 1826-LA) (E 'j and the Asia.k Development Bank (ADB) fi-
nanced Northern Transmission Line Project. Electricity is readily available
in Vientiane and availability is expanding in the Vientiane Plain, both of
which are served by the country's major existing grid. Champassak and Sara-
vane provinces in the South are supplied by the new Xeset hydro plant (para.
1.25), supplemented by seasonal imports from Thailand through a grid that is
growing. Only small amounts of electricity are produced and consumed in other
provinces. Outside these two areas, electric service consists of (i) scat-
tered small generating sets serving the small surrounding communities, and
(ii) in areas along the Mekong River in Savannakhet and Khammouane Provinces,
two interconnections with the Thai grid supplying power to the towns of Thak-
hek and Savannakhet, and surrounding communities (paras. 1.12 and 1.13).
Electricity Generation and Consumption
1.6       All major Lao electricity distribution grids are linked to the Thai
grid, either importing from or exporting to it. The present installed gener-
ating capacity in the Lao PDR is about 211 MW, consisting of about 200 MW of
hydro generation and the remainder of small diesel plants. Annex 1.1 shows
details of the location and capacity of the public electric services operated
in the various provinces. Power consumption within the Lao PDR, at about 50
kWh per person per year (1.989), is among the lowest in Asia. In addition, the
distribution network is limited, and only about 17% of tLe population current-
ly has access to electricity supplied from three small, separate grids that
are interconnected to the Thai system, but not to each other. This low con-
nection rate results from local supply constraints nationwide. Even where
grid supplies are available, networks can be extended to serve nearby, dis-
persed population pockets only when justified by economic activity. In turn,
the areas beyond the two ribbons of development (para. 1.1) have very limited
economic prospects; thus, extending the electric grids to serve those areas is
not expected to be economically feasible in the foreseeable future.
1.7       The 150 MW lam Ngu  hydroelectric station, which is operated by
EdL, i3 the most important facility in the power sector. Domestically, that
station supplies electricity to an integrated urban and rural electricity
system in the Vientiane Prefecture. From 1993 onward, Luang Prabang will be
integrated into this grid. However, most of Nam Ngum's production is exported
to Thailand.  Revenues from electricity sales to the Electricity Generating
Authority of Thailand (EGAT) in 1990 were about US$18 million.
1.8       In the late 1980s, the total energy generated annually at the Nam
Ngum hydro station ranged between 700 and 900 GWh, of which 130-160 GWh was
consumed locally and 500-700 GWh was exported to Thailand. Distribution loss-



- 3 -
es were about 30-40 GWh, which amounted to about 20X of domestic consumptlon.
Compared with the Vientiane area, electricity consumption in other areas of
the country is relatively small and accounts for about 20X of the total elec-
tricity sold within the Lao PJR. About 70X of the electricity consumed out-
side the Vientiane area is imported from the Thai grid. A summary of elec-
tricity generation and consumption in the Lao PDR for 1990 is presented in
Table 1.1, a more detailed account appears in Annex 1.2. Because of steadily
growing local demand, exports to Thailand have been decreasing from a high of
790 GWh in 1979 to about 600 GWh in 1990.

Local Electricity Service
1.9          EDL's main transmission system, which runs through the Vientiane
Prefecture, consists of 115 kV single circuic and double circuit transmission
lines (Map IBRD No. 23467). Both the double and single circuit lines transmit
energy from Nam Ngum, via the Phone Tong substation and the overhead Mekong
River crossing at Thanaleng, from where it connects to the Thai grid. From
the border, the lines travel within Thailand to the EGAT substations at Udon-
thani. A 22 kV distribution system, and 400/230 V distribution lines supply
electricity to consumers in Vientiane and surrounding areas.
1.10         In Luang Prabang Rrovince, electric power is being supplied from
the Nam Ndong 1,000 kW hydroelectric station, which was commissioned in 1971.
This is supplemented by a diesel station of 675 kW installed capacity. The
transmission line now under construction will connect the Vientiane area grid
with Luang Prabang town in 1993, expanding the integrated grid that will con-
tinue to be dominated by consumption in Vientiane. The inclusion of Luang
Prabang will add about 5 GWh per year to the approximately 170 GWh per year
then expected to be consumed in the Vientiane area. Consumption in Luang
Prabang is expected to grow quic'ly after interconnection. The current 3,500
consumers represent less than half of the potential connections in a town of
50,000 inhabitants. In addition to the growth expected in residential and
commercial demand, which is expected to reach about 11 GWh per year by the
late 1990s, an additional 18 GWh per year could be needed to supply a new me-
chanical workshop that is under construction in Luang Prabang and a new cement
plant planned for Vang "ieng. By the late 1990s, the annual generation re-



- 4 -
quired to supply domestlc needs from the Nam Ngum grid could amount to some
320 GWh of the 700 GWh projected annual production, leaving only about 380 GWh
for export to Thailand from this grid. ADB-financed investigations are now
under way to determine whether the two minor load centers of Savabouri and
Xiang Khoang, with aggregate demand of about 1 GWh per year, currently being
supplied by small diesel sets, should be connected to the grid.
1.11         SanAkhA ,   Paksa   .  These two isolated load centers, located
on the Mekong about 150 km upstream and downstream from Vientiane, are within
reasonable reach of EGAT substations at Loei and Bung Khan, respectively.
Sanakham, with about 1,500 households, has 250 connections being served by a
100 kW dissel set. A distribution substation across the Mekong is fully load-
ed, but the EGAT substation at Loei could be tapped with a 50 km tran.-4ssion
line.  Paksane town, with a population of about 28,000, is served by         0 kW
diesel se*, but could eventually consume about 6-7 GWh per year, wit!  . addi-
tional 6-l GWh consumption coming from productive uses and nearby settlements.
1.12         Thakhek.  The capital of Khammouane province is one of the two
load centers in central Laos that is connected to the Thai grid by underwater
Mekong River crossing links. The existing transmission line from EGAT's
Nakhon Phanom substation has a capacity of about 10 MW, and serves a current
peak demand of about 2.5 MW in Thakhek, mainly from residential and commercial
consumers. Recent consumption growth has been about 20X p.a., reaching about
5 GWh per year. That growth reflects rapid economic development of Thakhek
itself, and the southward expansion of the distribution network to serve small
load centers and the Se Bang Fai Plain, a center of irrigation investment.
Further growth in this expanding system, along with implementation of new
irrigation and industrial projects, could result in annual consumption of
about 25 GWh by the late 1990s, possibly supplemented by some 5-6 GWh from the
connection of a tin mining area in Phonetiou.
1.13         Savannakhlet.  This provincial capital and major load center is
connected to the Thai grid at its Mukdahan substation by an identical link to
that serving Thakhek; its peak demand is somewhat more than 4 Mti, and it cur-
rently imports about 18 GWh per year. Consumption growth during the 1980s was
about lOX p.a., and is likely to accelerate to reflect the recent spurt of
economic activity in the area. The expansion of the distribution network to
the east, north, and south was proceedirg steadily in any case, and it accel-
erated as a result of the availability of finance from SPE. With continued
growth and expansion, this network may reach a consumption level of 40-50 GWh
per year in the late 1990s, at which time the capacity of the transmission
link with Thailand will no longer be adequate.
1.14         Champassak/Saravane.  The economic development of these two dy-
namic southern provinces has been hampered by a lack of access to electricity.
Pakse, the capital of Champassak, has been dependent on the 2 MW Selabam hydro
plant, generating barely 5 GWh per year to serve a suppressed demand of per-
haps 7-8 GWh. Similarly, the town of Saravane and other smaller locations in
the area are supplied by small diesel sets that cover only part of potential
demand. The new Xeset hydro nower plant of 45 MW capacity, together with the
IDA-financed (i) export transmission line through Pakse, and (ii) ongoing pro-
vincial electrification program, will enable both provinces to increase s;-
plies substantially from 1991/92 onwards. By the year 2000, domestic demand
in the new grid is likely to reach 50 GWh per year.



-5-
1.15         The remaining southern provinces of Attopeu and Sekong are thinly
populated and remote, and are supplied by small diesel sets for a few hours
per day. Road access for diesel supply is difficult, particularly in the wet
seasan when the population center of Attopeu (about 40,000 inhabitants) is
completely isolated. The present supply of about 1 GWh per year in both prov-
inces is inadequate to satisfy the suppressed demand, which could reach about
5-6 GWh by the year 2000. The supply options are (i) the extension of the
Xeset grid to these two provinces, (ii) the development of small isolated
hydro supply, and (iii) the expansion of isolated diesel based supply, as
investments in roads make the delivery of diesel easier in the medium-term.
C. Power Sector Development Strategy
1.16         In the past, the development of the Lao power sector had been
troubled by the difficulty of integrating independent project proposals into
an internally consistent strategy. A multiplicity of project-specific studies
prevented easy comparison and prioritization, and the pro4eets that actually
were implemented were the ones for which the Government succeeded in raii.in,
concessional finance. In recent years, the Government and EDL have made sig-
nificant progress in formulating an integrated sector investment strategy.
1.17         1he Drinciples and criteria of EdL's investment strategy reflect
the importance of interlinkages with the nearby Thai grid. In comparison to
the small Lao power sector, the integrated EGAT system has about 8,000 MW of
installed capacity and generates about 45,000 GWh per year; it has been grow-
ing at close to 15% p.a. recently. This major system may reach retail sales
of about 100,000 GCh by the year 2000, and serve a peak demand of perhaps
16,000 to 19,000 MW. In view of tha close link between the Thai and Lao sys-
tems, and the relatively marginal nature of the individual Lao grids in rela-
tion to the dominant EGAT grid, investment planning on the Lao side should be
based on the following principles:
(a)    Large-scale proposed joint hydro projects of an international
character should be treated separately from the normal ongoing
EdL investment program, as they require different institutional
and financing techniques, and function essentially as enclaves or
border projects for the EGAT grid. However, EdL should be close-
ly involved in the preparation of these projects.
(b)    Medium-sized hydro sites within easy reach of existing grids
should be optimized with respect to lowest unit cost (presumably
corresponding to maximum economic installed capacity), in order
to provide competitive additions to exportable surplus from these
grids, and to ensure low-cost domestic supply in the long run.
(c)    Service to remote rural areas, accessible only through difficult
terrain, should only be provided by extending existing grids if
the economic development and suppressed observed demand of those
areas are sufficient for the connection to be economically justi-
fied. Supplying these areas with micro-hydro projects should be
considered with utmost caution, as the high unit cost of such
supplies often makes them uneconomic. Electrification invest-
ments can contribute significantly to integrated rural develop-



-6-
ment programs if they are well coordinated with other infra-
structure investments. In turn, such integrated rural develop-
ment programs do address issues of poverty alleviation.
(d)    Transmission and distribution investment should be tailored care-
fully to the modest needs of small, scattered load centers, by
avoiding premature investment in long domestic transmission
lines, and by seeking mediumi-term, lower cost options such as
improved cross-river l',nks with the EGAT grid.
Major Joint Projects
1.18         Potential large-scale hydro projects, entirely or partially on
Lao territory, but designed to serve mainly the Thai grid, require institu-
tional structures and financing mechanisms that extend beyond the capability
of the Lao economy alone. For this reason, they require a joint decision-
making process between Thailand and the Lao PDR (and whatever other parties
join in the financing arrangements or the riparian consultative process), and
thus need to be removed from the puraly Lao strategy formulation. They do,
however, have to be taken into account when they affect domestic decisions
concerning transmission and distribution investments. The main projects of
this type that could have an impact within the next 10-15 years are:
(a)    d^mTheun II.  This project has been investigated by a full fea-
sibility study, financed by UNDP and executed by IDA. The pro-
ject is located in Khammouane Province on the Nam Theun, a tribu-
tary of the Mekong, and can be designed for a maximum installed
capacity of 600 MW, with a first stage of 300 MW. The project
would be connected directly to the EGAT grid via a 75 km trans-
mission line within Laos, continuing through Thai territory to a
230 KV substation. The point of interconnection with the Thai
system, near Thakhek, is a Lao load center that could tap the
transmission line through a step down transformer, and thereby
replace the present supply from Thailand (para. 1.12). However,
before the project can be regarded as feasible, some important
environmental issues still need to be resolved.
(b)    Pa Mont.  This Mekong mainstream project has been under discus-
sion for many years, but progress has been limited by the inabil-
ity of the riparian countries to reach consensus on the major
issues. The project site is in Vientiane Province, just upstream
of Vientiane itself. The main issue is the need to resettle
large numbers of the upstream population. Recent designs envi-
sion a reservoir level of about 200-210 m, an estimate of about
2,700 MW of installed capacity, and the resettlement of about
60,000 people.
(c)    Nam NhieR.  This promising site in Bolikhamxay Province, on a
tributary of the Mekong, is being investigated with French tech-
nical assistance. Preliminary studies indicate a possible in-
stalled capacity of about 120-500 MW, and a unit cost of about
US30/kWh. The power would be transmitted to Thailand via a new
Mekong River crossing at Paksane.



-7-
Domestic System Development
1.19         The main criteria underlying an investment strategy aimed at
addressing internal Lao needs should be (i) minimization of the unit cost of
medium-sized hydro projects, and (ii) adoption of least-cost approaches to
supplying new or growing load centers. Therefore, the sector has to be treat-
ed as three major grid clusters that will grow together only gradually. The
three clusters are the Northern, Central, and Southern groups of load centers;
each has separate established and potential sources of supply. The Northern
System is centered around the Nam Ngum grid; the Central System in Khammouane
and Savannakhet draws supplies from Thailand, and would benefit if Nam Theun
II is implemented; and the Southern System is currently being supplied by the
recently completed Xeset hydro plant and the much smaller Selabam hydro plant,
both run of the river schemes, the output of which need to be supplemented
with imports from the Thai grid during the dry season.
1.20         Given the local demand potential and supply options, the medium-
term development of these three grid clusters should combine network expansion
to meet the growing needs of existing consumers and connect new consumers,
with the maintenance of export capabilities. Such a program can be developed
and implemented independently of decisions concerning major joint projects
such as Nam Theun II. Annex 1.3 provides EdL's ten-year investment program.
The early years are dominated by expenditures for ongoing projects, while new
investment will only be ready for implementation as of late 1992. Excluding
major export projects, EdL's annual investment expenditure will grow to and
stabilize at about US$25-30 million per year during the mid and late 1990s.
1.21         In the Northern System, the ongoing projects are (i) the trans-
mission line between Nam Ngum and Luang Prabang, (ii) rehabilitation of Nam
Ngum units 3 and 4, and (iii) distribution investments in Vientiane proper and
the Vientiane Plain. The future expansion program for this area (pending
establishment of the economic feasibility of constituent projects), which
would increase the system's installed capacity by about 50X, includes:
-      Nam Leuk/Nam Song water diversion and hydro generation projects,
to be commissioned in the late 1990s;
-      Extension of Nam Ngum hydro plant by one 40 MW unit, also to be
commissioned in the late 1990s;
-      A Third Vientiane Plain Rural Electrification Project;
-      A Vientiane Town Distribution Project;
-      Transmission lines from the Vientiane/Luang Prabang grid to Saya-
bouri and Xiang Khoang; and
-      A new river crossing link with the Thai grid at Paksane.
1.22         The ongoing program for the Central System includes the comple-
tion of works in Savannakhet being financed by SPE, and some minor additional
distribution works. Apart from Nam Theun II and its associated export trans-
mission line, future investments in this area that would accommodate normal
demand growth and add modest extensions to the distribution system, include:



- 8 -
-      Khammouane Province distribution network expansion;
-      Savannakhet Province distribution network expansion; and
-      Rehabilitation end strengthening of the two river crossing links
to the Thai system at Savannakhet and Thakhek, and establishment
of a new link at Se Bang Fai.
1.23         In the Southern System, the completion of the Xeset project and
the electrification of Champassak/Saravane will create the basis of an inte-
grated grid with exportable surplus. The next medium-term investments (sub-
ject to confirmation of their economic feasibility), will involve the expan-
sion of distribution networks to supply growing load centers in Champassak,
Saravane, Attopeu, and Sekong provinces. In the long run, the Southern System
as a whole may benefit from the development of major hydro sites in the Sekong
River basin. These sites would have to be optimized as export projects, with
power being evacuated to Thailand via a reinforced Southern grid. As the
potential sites have only been identified and not yet studied, implementation
of this option would not be likely before the turn of the century.
D. Major Sector Issues
1.24         The Lao power sector faces three major issues that need to be
addressed in the context of the proposed and future projects:
(a)    Pricing and Finance.  The Government's past policy had been to
cross-subsidize domestic consumption with export revenues. How-
ever, export prices have declined and domestic electricity con-
sumption has increased, further decreasing export revenues. As
the available margin for cross-subsidization declines, domestic
tariff revenues will have to cover an increasing share of EdL's
financial requirements. During the next few years, significant
increases in domestic tariff levels will be needed to satisfy
both EdL's financial performance targets, and its objective of
approaching the recovery of economic costs (paras. 5.15 - 5.20).
(b)    Institutional Improvement.  EdL is a competent utility.  However,
it has only limited depth of skilled managers and professionals,
which will be taxed severely by (i) the utility's own growth, and
(ii) t'is acquisition of the provincial utilities. Although on-
going consultancies included under SPE are providing the frame-
work for decentralization and formalized systems and procedures,
EdL needs to implement this framework expeditiously, refine the
processes, and provide staff training at all levels. Similarly,
it needs to ensure that the recent improvements in system loss
reduction are sustained in Vientiane and replicated in the prov-
inces under EdL's jurisdiction; and that all consumer groups
throughout EdL's various service areas are properly metered and
billed (para. 3.6 - 3.9).
(c)    Investment Strategy.  Given the diverse objectives for sector
development (export enhancement and grid expansion), the sector's
investment strategy has to be re-evaluated regularly, taking



. 9 -
account of changing circumstances in the Thai power market and
the pace of development of domestic load centers. This will
require strong analytical and planning skills, and maturity and
deftness in international negotiations (paras. 1.16 - 1.23).
E. Past Bank Group Involvement in the Sector
1.25         The Bank Group has had sporadic involvement in the Lao power
sector since the late 1960s. Bank Group assistance began with administration
of a development fund, financed by eight countries, to construct the first
phase of the Nam Ngum Hydroelectric Project (2 x 15 MW units); this activity
was completed in 1971. Ten years later in 1982, IDA provided a US$15 million
Credit to finance the Nam Ngum Hydroelectric Project (Credit 1197-LA). This
project involved construction of the third phase of the Nam Ngum power plant,
including Inter alla the installation of the fifth unit of 40 MW capacity.
IDA's involvement in that project was an important catalyst for formalizing a
broadly revised power sales agreement between the Lao PDR and Thailand. Then,
in 1987, IDA provided about US$25.8 million equivalent to finance the foreign
cost of SPE, which itself was the transmission and distribution component of a
broader program to develop electricity resources and supply in Champassak,
Saravane and Savannakhet provinces. SPE is expected to be completed within
the schedule and cost estimate developed at appraisal. The Xeset Hydroelec-
tric Project, financed by ADB, UNDP and the Swedish International Development
Agency (SIDA), provided the generation component of that regional program.
1.26         The Bank Group's previous involvement has shown that: (i) while
EdL is effective at implementing IDA-financed projects, its thin staff needs
to be supplemented with technical assistance; (ii) EdL, as the repository of
the sector's technical expertise, needs control over the development, imple-
mentation, and operation of the systems being developed under internationally
financed projects; and (iii) to enable EdL to cope with the growing complexity
and geographical dispersion of its business, past informal practices for man-
aging operations and implementing projects need to be supplemented with more
formal systems and procedures and modern technology and communications facili-
ties. To address these lessons of the past, the proposed project includes:
(i) a project management consultancy (para. 3.15); (ii) a provision that EdL
acquire the provincial utilities that will receive benefits from the proposed
project and that it does not already own (para. 2.5); and (iii) a far-reaching
institutional development program (para. 3.9), which builds on the efforts
begun under SPE and has a special emphasis on developing skills and capabili-
ties at the provincial level. Moreover, although the implementation of these
remedial measures is the subject of dated action programs under the proposed
project, EdL has a satisfactory record for implementing such programs under
previous projects. The proposed project is an integral building block of the
sector investment strategy (paras. 1.16 and 1.23).
F. Rationale for Continuing Involvement
1.27         IDA's involvement in the proposed project would be an important
catalyst with regard to the following objectives:



- 10 -
(a)    By providing financial services to both EdL and EGAT, IDA has ac-
tively been promoting cooperation between the Thai and Lao power
systems. The proposed project will support an increased number
of improved interconnections between the systems, and the devel-
opment of additional medium sized hydro generation with export
potential via existing links. In addition, IDA will continue its
efforts to foster the use of improved institutional, pricing, and
financing practices between the two systems;
(b)    By financing the next phase of distribution network expansion in
the central and southern regions, IDA would continue assisting
the Government's efforts to fine-tune its sector investment
strategy, focusing on optimization of the domestic transmission
and distribution networks;
(c)    By supporting the proposed project, IDA would assist the Govern-
ment and EdL in formulating and implementing a rational electric-
ity tariff policy, aimed at enhancing the share of EdL's revenues
being provided by local consumers; and
(d)    By assisting with the next phase of institutional development,
IDA would promote the upgrading of EdL's skills and capabilities,
and its operational and financial effectiveness.



- 11 -
2. THE BENEFICIARY
A. Background
2.1       The Beneficiary of the proposed Credit will be Electricite du Laos
(EdL), a wholly state-owned enterprise responsible for all generation and
transmission, and some distribution of electricity within the Lao PDR.
2.2       Prior to 1975, EdL was a public corporation responsible for gener-
ating and distributing electricity throughout the country. After the present
Government came to power, EdL became a state enterprise reporting to the Min-
istry of Industry and Handicrafts (MOI). In the recent past, EdL has served
mainly as an executing and operating agency, with EdL's parent ministry having
responsibility for sector planning and policy making; approval of the Council
of Ministers has been required for most planning and policy making decisions
and even some operating decisions. Since 1986, the Government has evolved a
policy to give greater autonomy to larger enterprises such as EdL. While the
Government still retains full control over electricity tariffs, EdL has been
given broad responsibility to manage its technical and commercial operations,
and planning, and to set most of the policies that govern its internal and
external activities.
2.3       Historically, EdL has been responsible for operation of the Nam
Ngum power plant, delivery of electricity to Thailand at 115 kV, and distribu-
tion of electricity throughout Vientiane and surrounding rural areas. Elec-
tricity outside the Vientiane area was supplied and retailed by provincial
electric power authorities, which operated independently of EdL for most of
their affairs. The provincial and local governments made policy for these
authorities; and EdL usually managed the construction of fixed assets on their
behalf. Although the formal ties were loose, these provincial authorities
drew heavily on EdL whenever any technical expertise was necessary.
2.4       At appraisal of both SPE and the ADB-financed Xeset Hydroelectric
Project, the institutional capabilities of the provincial authorities were
found to be very limited; and a program was developed to broaden substantially
EdL's role outside Vientiane. EdL was given the responsibility to construct,
operato, and maintain the Xeset plant and the 115 kV transmission line linking
Xeset to Pakse and from there to the Thai border. While EdL would build the
distribution networks included under SPE, the Government would ensure that
they would be operated and maintained properly by arranging for EdL to assume
functional control over tne distribution utilities in the recipient provinces
(Savannakhet, Khammouane, Champassak, and Saravane) by June 30, 1991, a few
months before the expected completion of those networks. Later, the Govern-
ment agreed that EdL should also assume functional control over the Luang
Prabang provincial authority, the beneficiary of the ADB-financed Northern
Transmission Line Project, not later than June 30, 1991. In effect, the Gov-
ernment recognized that plans to decentralize the electric utilities were
effectively undermined by the dearth of qualified technical personnel, espe-
cially in the provinces; and through this device of functional control, EdL
could ensure the operational and commercial efficiency of these utilities.



- 12 -
2.5       In early 1991, the Lao Government decided to go beyond the func-
tional control approach, and accept the proposal of the concerned provincial
governments that EdL purchase outright the assets, liabilities and businesses
of those five provincial electricity authorities. The terms of purchase were
agreed on a case by case basis during April-June 1991, and the purchases them-
selves became effective during June 1991. This change to outright purchase
more than meets the objectives for functional control, and therefore was ac-
ceptable to both IDA and ADB. Two additional provinces (Bolikhamxay and Se-
kong) with independent electricity authorities are expected to receive bene-
fits under the proposed project. At negotiations, the Government agreed that
it will arrange for EdL to acquire the assets, liabilities and businesses of
the provincial electricity authorities serving consumers in Bolikhamxay and
Sekong provinces, prior to the onset of construction of the networks being
financed under the proposed project.
2.6       A_ the Lao Government has increasingly entrusted important economic
activities to the private sector, questions have arisen about the extent to
which the electricity sector might be privatized. While several private com-
panies are now selling materials and equipment for electricity supply, EdL
could not realistically be sold or otherwise transferred to the private sector
at this time. EdL is facing a major investment program (para. 4.21) over the
next ten years, and only the Government has the resource mobilization capacity
to meet EdL's projected capital requirements. Also, EGAT's attitude toward
importing electricity from EdL is largely a reflection of bilateral relations
between Thailand and the Lao PDR. Moreover, EdL is not ready for privatiza-
tion;.as a first step in the lengthy process, increasing ErLL's autonomy and
its corporatization should be primary objectives for the next five years. The
developmaent of sound managerial processes that enable accountability to pri-
vate shareholders is one important objective of the institutional development
component of the proposed projuct (para. 3.9).
B. Organization
Structure
2.7       EdL is headed by a General Manager who is appointed by decree of
the Council of Ministers on the recommendation of the Minister of Industry and
Handicrafts. He reports to the Minister, usually through a Vice Minister who
has specific supervisory responsibility for EdL. He is assisted by a Deputy
General Manager, whose appointment also has the endorsement of the Council of
Ministers. The allocation of responsibility between the two is not clearly
delineated in the organization chart; thus, the actual division of responsi-
bility is determined by informal arrangement between the two incumbents.
2.8       EdL is currently modifying its organization structure.  The present
organization chart is shown in Arnnex 2.1, and the proposed structure in Annex
2.2. Under the existing structure, 25 separate departments report to the
General Manager's office. As EdL's scope of activity has become broader and
more complex, control over the existing structure has become increasingly
difficult. The new structure is more stratified, with only two Branches and
six departments reporting to the General Manager's office. The Branch manag-
ers are responsible for geographically dispersed functional activities, while
the various departments provide staff services.



- 13 -
2.9       The major institutional development program included under SPE pro-
vided EdL with (i) basic systems and on the job training in accounting and
finance; (ii) computer hardware, software and training, as well as rudimentary
information systems; and (iii) organizational systems, procedures and manuals.
As EdL's activities become increasingly complex and geographically dispersed,
follow-up programs are needed to (i) monitor skills acquired by EdL's staff
under SPE; (ii) provide EdL with more detailed, broadened systems and proce-
dures; (iii) consolidate the activities of EdL and its provincial subsidiar-
ies; and (iv) provide on-the-job training to headquarters and provincial
staff. A comprehensive technical assistance program featuring follow-up con-
sultancies for Organization and Management (O&M Study), and Finance, Account-
ing and Computerization (Finance Study) has been included in the proposed
project (para 3.4).
Personnel and Stamng
2.10      As of November 1991, EdL's total personnel included 1,596 employ-
ees, with training levels as follow: engineers (177); technicians (non-engi-
neering college graduates) (277); skilled labor (930); unskilled labor and
others (212). The last category includes drivers, typists, junior accoun-
tants, public relations specialists, nurses, teachers, and shop employees. Of
the total, about 1003 had jobs at EdL headquarters or elsewhere in Vientiane.
The remaining 573 are employed in through EdL's provincial subsidiaries (main-
ly in Champassak and Savannakhet). The distribution of staff by department
and skills level is given in Annex 2.3. EdL is reviewing its staffing comple-
ment to ensure consistency with the Government's efficiency objectives, and
may allow some attrition, if deemed appropriate.
Training
2.11      EdL currently operates a training center through which employees,
deemed to be skilled, learn a variety of jobs. The program is a standard one
that lasts two years and focusses on the technical aspects of the EdL opera-
tions. In the program, employees receive training similar to an engineering
curriculum, while also learning about the EdL network and operation and main-
tenance of EdL's equipment. All existing employees described as "skilled
labor" have completed the EdL. vocational school curriculum.
2.12      During 1991, using French Government assistance, Electricite de
France (EdF) conducted a review of the training center's facilities and pro-
gram, and found that significant upgrades were needed. The French Government
will finance a small package of the most urgently needed equipment, while the
remaining complement that has been agreed with the Government has been includ-
ed for purchase under the proposed project (para. 3.9 [d]). Similarly, in
conjunction with the appraisal of the proposed project, the training program
was found to need broadening to cover the variety of technical skills needed
by the evolving EdL. These training program upgrades will be provided through
(i) a technical cooperation arrangement with Tenaga Nasional Berhad (TNB) of
Malaysia; and (ii) technical assistance (para. 3.9 [d]). Both of these train-
ing components would be financed by the proposed credit.
2.13      Tata Consulting Engineers, Ltd. (TCE) of India has been employed by
EdL since 1981, Inter alia, to review and revise the organization's accounting
procedures and to help implement appropriate accounting systems. Although the



- 1& -
accounting function had become marginally satisfactory prior to the acquisi-
tion of the provincial subsidiaries, considerable further technical assistance
is needed to enable (i) upgrading to acceptable standards of the accounting
function at the provincial subsidiaries; and (ii) mastery by headquarters
staff of the techniques involved in consolidating the accounts of disag-
gregated units. The consultancy for finance and accounting is expected to
continue providing training to EdL's accounts staff.
Operation, Maintenance and Losses
2.14      Although it is now about 20 years old, the Nam Ngum power station
has relatively recent equipment that has been kept in good repair. The Xeset
plant was commissioned in 1991, and has modern equipment which is being oper-
ated properly and efficiently. EdL has a history of conducting regular main-
tenance according to generally accepted public utility practices.
2.15      The EdL system includes only 250 km of 115 kV transmission line,
which is used to evacuate to Thailand about 79X of the energy generated from
Nam Ngum and, when steady state operation is reached, a similar proportion of
energy from Xeset. Losses within the transmission system have been contained
at a satisfactory level of about 21. The distribution system that supplies
Nam Ngum energy to local consumers is also small, and covers areas limited to
within the Vientiane Prefecture and the Vientiane Plain. Much of these dis-
tribution facilities were built during the last 15-18 years. The system it-
self is modern and can support considerable expansion. In view of these fa-
vorable characteristics, the distribution system's losses that were recorded
at about 23X during appraisal of SPE were considered excessive, and EdL agreed
under that project to implement an acceptable loss reduction program. That
program realized limited progress through 1990; however, once EdL received new
meter testing and calibration equipment, the level of distribution losses
dropped sharply. In 1991, system losses averaged only about 16X. Despite
this progress, EdL believes it still has substantial scope for further reduc-
tion of system losses, and an action program to accomplish that objective is
included under the proposed project (para. 3.7).
C. Financial Management
Accounting and Budgeting
2.16      EdL's accounting system is based on conventional double-entry prin-
ciples, with income, expenses, assets and liabilities being classified accord-
ing to a code of accounts determined by the Government. Basic accounting
records were first compiled in 1979, and then consisted of a general ledger, a
cash book, and sales journals for EdL's retail operations and company stores.
In 1982, TCE reviewed the accounting system and issued a report regarding
control records and procedures. Most of the recommendations have been imple-
mented. Recently, the system was adapted for consistency with the Govern-
ment's new accounting system. Quarterly financial statements, consisting of a
balance sheet and profit and loss statement, are now being prepared regularly.
2.17      EdL prepares annual capital and operating budgets which are re-
viewed with MOI, the Ministry of Economy, Plan and Finance and the State Plan-
ning Committee in June-July of the year preceding the one for which they will
be in effect. The budgets are then approved by the Council of Ministers dur-



- 1S -
ing October-November. Neither periodic budget reviews nor variance analyses
are conducted during the financial year itself.
Commercial Systems
2.18      EdL has substantially improved its commercial systems since the ap-
praisal of SPE. At that time, the turnover of accounts receivable was aver-
aging an unsatisfactory 150 days (net of unread meter accruals averaging 30
days); however, the turnover rate for sales within the Lao PDR was more than
one year with the aging of receivables bearing similar characteristics among
Government and private sector consumers. As a result, EdL was asked to agree
to implement an action program to reduce accounts receivable to commercially
appropriate levels. This program was relatively successful, so that the turn-
over rate currently averages about 56 days (net of unread meter accruals).
More importantly, the turnover rate on sales within the Lao PDR has been re-
duced to about 90-100 days (net of unread meter accruals).
2.19      EdL has made substantial progress in computerizing its billing and
collection systems, which represent a substantial improvement over past prac-
tices. During the past few years, one important effort in the Vientiane area
has been for EdL to identify and meter all consumers who draw electricity from
its distribution networks. Now, EdL needs to ensure that they are all sys-
tematically being billed. In March 1992, EdL confirmed that all electricity
consumers in the Vientiane area have been incorporated into the billing and
collection system, and are being billed regularly. For the provincial subsid-
iaries to use the new processes effectively, (i) the computerized systems need
to be adapted to the circumstances of the subsidiaries, and (ii) provincial
staffs will need to be trained in their use. Technical assistance to help EdL
adapt these new commercial processes for provincial use is included in the
Finance Study under the proposed project. Assuming EdL invests the time and
effort needed to strengthen impleuentation of these new systems, the turnover
rate on local sales will drop still further in coming months, both in Vien-
tiane and at the subsidiaries.
2.20      Billing and collection arrangements with regard to exports to Thai-
land are enumerated in the long-term power sales agreements between EdL and
EGAT. They call for a 45 day payment period on top of an unread meter accrual
that has averaged about 30 days. Payment is to be made in US dollars into a
special account at the Thai Military Bank, which then remits the proceeds to
the Banque pour le Commerce Exterieur Lao, which in turn credits the proceeds
net of export taxes to EdL. These combined banking transactions can take as
many as 15 days. Thus, although EdL carries in accounts receivable an average
of slightly less than 90 days of sales to EGAT, the period could not be short-
ened substantially; moreover, because these payments have followed contrac-
tually stipulated terms, the corresponding accounts receivable cannot be con-
sidered as "in arrears".
Audit
2.21      Responsibility for auditing EdL's financial statements belongs to
(i) the Department of Finance within MOI, and (ii) the Department of Accounts
of the Ministry of Economy, Planining and Finance. The audits include inspec-
tions of production operations, fixed assets, planning, and financial activi-



- 16 -
ties. They also establish the degree of compliance with labor conditions and
review wages and workers' living conditions. Under SPE, to accommodate the
heavy backlog facing the Government's auditors, IDA agreed that EdL should
furnish the reports of these audits to IDA within nine months of the end of
the financial year, and it has regularly complied with this agreement. The
Government's auditors continue to experience delays in completing audits be-
cause their backlog remains heavy. Therefore, at negotiations, EdL again
agreed to furnish audited financial statements to IDA within nine months of
the end of each financial year.
2.22      Although EdL recognizes the desirability of baving an internal au-
dit unit, little progress has been made in this direction. The major con-
straint is the dearth of adequately trained staff. Currently, internal checks
conducted by the accounts department are restricted to periodic verifications
of cash, stores, consumables, and other movable assets such as office equip-
ment, furniture and vehicles. These checks are sufficient for the time being.
The development of an internal audit unit with a broader scope of work must
necessarily follow the institutional upgrades that will result from the pro-
posed project's O&M and Finance Studies.
Taxes and Dividends
2.23      In the past, EdL's entire cash surplus from operations had been
payable to the Government.  However, under current practice, EdT pays substan-
tial taxes, including: (i) a Local Turnover Tax of 3% on all sales within the
Lao PDR (the rate is expected to increase to 5% in 1992); (ii) an export tax,
levied upon remittance to the Lao PDR, of 20% on exports (the rate is expected
to drop to 10% in 1992); and (iii) an income tax on EdL's surplus after inter-
est charges. In addition, the Government has been providing for the recovery
of the cost of power sector investments by requiring that EdL transfer 46% of
depreciation; the Government is considering replacing this transfer with a
dividend of similar magnitude. Thereafter, EdL retains its after tax surplus.
Insurance
2.24      In the absence of insurance companies, EdL makes no specific provi-
sions for insuring its assets. The Government, as the owner of all assets
managed by state enterprises, is responsible for meeting casualty losses wh'.ch
cannot be covered from the enterprise's normal revenues, and this arrangement
has previously been acceptable to IDA. For project related assets, EdL has
arranged for contractors for civil works and suppliers of materials take out
appropriate insurance coverage during the construction period. These arrange-
ments, which were used under the Nam Ngum and SPE projects, are expected to be
replicated under the proposed project.



- 17 -
3. THE PROJECT
A. Origin and Status of Preparation
3.1       With the Nam Ngum hydro plant and the two ADB-financed Vientiane
Plain Rural Electrification Projects (VPRE), grid supplies have been a cata-
lyst fir economic development in Vientiane and the areas immediately to the
north. Economic activity in the central and southern regions, however, has
lagged because infrastructure (notably electricity supply) has not kept pace
with developmental needs. Grid supplies in that area have gradually broadened
with (i) the completion in May 1991, of the ADB-financed 45 MW hydro plant at
Xeset and (ii) EdL's progress in implementing SPE.
3.2       To continue its efforts to increase the availability of electricity
to developments in the Mekong River environs, EdL retained TCE in 1990 to
identify and prepare projects for the further electrification of (i) tbh cen-
tral provinces of Savannakhet, Khammouane and Bolikhamxay; (ii) the southern
provinces of Champassak, Saravane, Sekong and Attopeu; and (iii) the Xanakham
District of Vientiane Province. TCE's studies included a 10-year load fore-
cast for each province, a technical evaluation of alternative electrification
plans, and economic evaluations for each scheme. TCE's finalized report was
the basis of this proposed project.
B. Objectives
3.3       The objectives of the proposed project are:  (i) extending elec-
tricity supply to more consumers in the Southern and Central Regions of the
Lao PDR, including areas within the provinces of Champassak, Saravane, Sekong,
Savannakhet, Khammouane, and Bolikhamxay (Map IBRD No. 23467); (ii) enhancing
the quality of supply for existing consumers in those provinces; (iii) devel-
oping additional power export prospects for the Lao PDR; (iv) improving EdL's
system efficiency; and (v) continuing EdL's institutional development.
C. Project Description
3.4       The proposed project includes:
(a)  Central Region ElectrIfIcatIon.  Construction of: (i) a new 115 kV
double-circuit overhead river-crossing line with associated substa-
tion at Savannakhet, and a new 22 kV double-circuit overhead river-
crossing line at Thakhek (both of which would replace aging under-
water cables), to interconnect with the Thai grid; (ii) new 22 kV
single-circuit overhead river-crossing lines to interconnect with
the Thai grid at Se Bang FaiaV and Paksane, each with an associated
2/ Using a river crossing line to interconnect the proposed Se Bang Fai
network with the EGAT grid was found to have modest cost advantages over
the use of an overland 22 kV interconnection with the Savannakhet sub-
station (about 40 km. away). However, because some topographical prob-
(continued...)



- 18 -
22 kV substationJ'; and (iii) extension of the 22 kV distribution
networks in Savannakhet, Khammouane and Bolikhamxay provinces;
(b) Southern Region Electr1f1catLon. Extension of the 22 kV distribu-
tion networks to additional areas in Champassak, Sekong, and Sara-
vane provinces;
(c)  Improving Svstem Efflclenc .  This component includes (i) implemen-
tation of an action program for EdL to monitor and reduce system
losses, increase collections, and improve the billing practices in
the Vientiane area and its provincial subsidiaries; and (ill provi-
sion and installation of equipment and materials (includin6 spare
parts) to strengthen EdL's operation and maintenance services, par-
ticularly at its provincial subsidiaries;
(d)  Continuation of EdL's Institutlonal Development.  Provision of
equipment, material and services, and technical assistance for up-
grading the skills of EdL's staff and its training capabilities;
and
(e)  Future ProJect Develogment.  Preparation of a feasibility and de-
sign study for (i) the installation of additional capacity at the
Nam Ngum hydro station, together with (ii) any needed strengthening
of the transmission line from Nam Ngum to the Thai border.
Provincial Electrification
3.5       The scope of work for the electrification of the central and south-
ern provinces would include about 850 km of 22 kV medium voltage lines and
spurs; about 950 km of 400 V distribution feeders; one new 115 kV substation
and two new 22 kV substations for the interconnection with the Thai grid;
about 300 22/.4 kV step down transformers with a total capacity of around 35
MVA; housewiring; and needed connections to electrify some 500 villages with a
total of about 14,500 households (Annex 3.1 provides a detailed breakdown by
province). Villages to be electrified have been selected on the basis of
economic criteria (paras. 5.6 - 5.8), including (i) their proximity to the 22
kV lines; (ii) the number of households in the village; and (iii) the number
of irrigation pumps, rice mills and other industrial activities expected to be
connected to the system.

river crossing line could negate its cost advantages, an understanding
was reached at negotiations to reconsider the use of the overland
scheme. EdL is studying the details of the two approaches, and will
report its findings in late 1992. If the overland scheme can be built
at comparable cost with a comparable rate of return and without impor-
tant environmental side effects, that approach would be substituted for
the river crossing at Se Bang Fai.

river-crossings to its grid at its own cost, and would also provide free
of cost to EdL a transformer for the 115 kV substation at Savannakhet.

Improving System Efficiency
3.6       Under SPE, EdL agreed to implement an action program to reduce dis-
tribution losses in the Vientiane area. Actions taken by EdL to date include:
(i) establishment of a task force headed by a senior EdL engineer, who reports
directly to the General Manager; (ii) identification of losses by installing
master meters, which are monitored and analyzed on a monthly basis, at all key
points within its networks; (iii) establishment of a meter testing laboratory
and replacement of faulty meters; and (iv) installat! n of office equipment
and a computerized billing system. So far, the program has yielded commend-
able results, including: (i) a major reduction in distribution losses from 23X
in 1985 to almost 16% in 1991; (ii) the metering of all consumption in the
Vientiane area; and (iii) the development of a computer program to identify
and diagnose system losses as early as possible. A breakdown of system losses
for 1985-90 according to station use, transmission losses, and distribution
losses is shown in Annex 3.2.
3.7       Despite this commendable progress, distribution losses of 161 are
still too high. About half of these losses might result from non-technical
causes. Therefore, the loss reduction program needs to be continued under the
proposed project, not only in the Vientiane area but also in the service areas
of EdL's provincial subsidiaries. EdL should also focus on reducing unbilled
power and unpaid bills. An action plan is given in Annex 3.3. At negotia-
tions, EdL agreed to implement an action program to (i) monitor and reduce
system losses; (ii) increase its collection efficiency; and (iii) improve its
implementation of the recently computerized billing system, especially in its
provincial service areas.
3.8       To improve system efficiency still further, the project provides
for EdL to acquire (i) spare parts for its generation and transmission facili-
ties; (ii) tools, vehicles, and radio equipment; (iii) micro-computers to be
used primarily for upgrading the inputs to and utilization of EdL's Management
Information System (MIS); and (iv) a telecommunication system that meets EdL's
communications requirements within the central and southern regions as well as
between EdL's Vientiane headquarters and key locations within the provinces.
An indicative list of this equipment and material is shown in Annex 3.4.
Continuation of EdL's Institutional Development
3.9       The project's institutional development component includes:
(a)  a Finance. Accounting and Computerization Consultancy (Finance
Study) that would train finance staff at EdL headquarters and the
provincial subsidiaries, and assist them in implementing (i) EdL's
accounting system (especially -he closing and consolidation of
year-end accounts) and (ii) financial processes related to EdL's
new MIS. This activity is expected to involve about 60 person-
months of consulting inputs;
(b)  an Organization and Management Study (O&M Study) that would contin-
ue the work begun under SPE to develop the policies, regulations,
systems, procedures, and guidelines needed to enable EdL to func-
tion as a decentralized national utility. This activity is expect-
ed to involve about 30 person-months of consulting inputs;



- 20 -
(c) a Computerized Rural Electrification Planning System that would
include the purchase of appropriate computer hardware and software,
and associated technical assistance, to model the selection and
evaluation of projects for electrifying new areas. This would as-
sist EdL in screening, ranking, and appraising investment options
that involve extensions of electric networks to supply consumers in
unserved areas within reach of the grid; and
(d) a Training Upgrading Program that would include: (i) the provision
of equipment and tools; (ii) the services of training experts; and
(iii) short-term secondments and external training, to acquaint
EdL's staff at all levels with the practices being implemented suc-
cessfully at other utilities in the region. The proposed project
also envisions that EdL would enter into a "technical cooperation
arrangement" with one of the stronger utilities in the region, TNB
of Malaysia. TNB has expressed its willingness to develop such a
relationship pending an exchange of visits between TNB and EdL man-
agers that would settle the particulars of the arrangement.
3.10      Draft scopes of work, terms of reference, and particulars of the
aforementioned technical assistance and training upgrades are detailed in
Annexes 3.5, 3.6, 3.7 and 3.8. At negotiations, EdL agreed that it would:
(i) by December 31, 1992, retain consultants, according to terms of reference
and following a selection procedure acceptable to IDA, to provide technical
assistance with regard to finance and accounting; (ii) by September 30, 1993,
retain consultants, according to terms of reference and following a selection
procedure acceptable to IDA, to perform a management and organization study;
(iii) by December 31, 1994, discuss with IDA the principal conclusions of
these consultancies; and (iv) according to a schedule acceptable to IDA, im-
plement those of the consultants' recommendations that are acceptable to the
Government, EdL and IDA. In addition, at negotiations, EdL agreed that it
will conclude a satisfactory technical cooperation arrangement with an accept-
able electric utility from the region.
Nam Ngum Hydro Station Extension
3.11      The further extension of the 150 MW Nam Ngum hydro station depends
on implementation of upst._am water diversion schemes at Nam Song and Nam
Leuk, which would increase and stabilize the flow of water into the Nam Ngum
reservoir. A prefeasibility study of these projects was completed under an
ADB-financed technical assistance grant; and a feasibility and design study
for the diversion schemes has recently begun and should be completed by mid
1992. If the results of that latter study are positive, ADB would appraise
these projects in late 1992. Based on the prefeasibility study, the water
diversion schemes would provide sufficient water flows into the Nam Ngum res-
ervoir to enable the addition of up to 50 MW of capacity at the Nam Ngum hydro
station. This will need to be confirmed by the ongoing feasibility study,
which will Inter alia address the hydrological factors involved in expanding
the capacity of the Nam Ngum plant. To optimize the benefits of the water
diversion schemes, any extension of the Nam Ngum plant should be implemented
in parallel with implementation of those schemes. Therefore, the proposed
project provides for a feasibility and design study for the works needed to
expand the Nam Ngum plant, and to strengthen (as needed) the transmission line
from Nam Ngum to the Thai border. This study is tentatively estimated to need



- 21 -
consultants' inputs of about 45 person-months. Draft terms of reference are
given in Annex 3.9; they will be finalized by EdL, in cooperation with IDA, as
soon as the results of the feasibility study tor the water diversion schemes
are available. At negotiations, EdL agreed that it would: (i) by September
30, 1993, retain consultants according to terms of reference and following a
selection procedure acceptable to IDA, to conduct a feasibility and design
study for extending the Nam Ngum power plant; and (ii) by December 31, 1994,
discuss the findings of the study with IDA.

feeders, 5% for all other works and 3% for services. Price contingencies for
foreign costs are calculated on the basis of the projected international rate
at 3.9% per annum for 1992-97. Price contingencies for local costs are calcu-
lated at 6.5% for 1992, and 5% per year for 1993 and thereafter.

E. Financing Plan
3.14      The proposed Credit of US$36 million would finance about 73% of the
estimated total financing requirement (100% of the foreign cost incurred with-
in the Lao PDR, net of interest during construction). EGAT would finance the
full value of the connecting links within Thailand, about half of the cost of
the Mekong River crossings, and the cost of one transformer for the 115 kV
substation at Savannakhet. Because the interconnections to EGAT are needed to
provide electricity for all the proposed central region networks, assurances
of EGAT's willingness to complete its portion of those interconnections is a
prerequisite to any EdL investments in those facilities. Thus, EdL affirmed
it would enter into a contractual arrangement with EGAT as a condition of
disbursement for the central region's networks. EdL would finance the full
US$6.9 million equivalent estimated local cost of the project, and an esti-
mated US$4.2 million equivalent (US$3.8 million equivalent of local cost and
US$0.4 million equivalent of foreign cost) for interest during construction.
The US$6.9 million equivalent that EdL would contribute to the project repre-
sents about 15% of the estimated project cost; the full US$11.1 million that
EdL is expected to finance represents about 23% of the total financing re-
quirement. The financing plan for the project is shown in Table 3.2.

F. Engineering and Implementation
3.15      The provincial electrification project component will be supervised
and managed by EdL's project office, with the assistance from consulting engi-
neers whose services would include: (i) finalization and/or review of designs;
(ii) preparation of bidding documents; (iii) conduct of bid evaluations;
(iv) arrangements for contracts; and (v) construction supervision. In view of
the experience EdL gained during the implementation of the two VPRE Projects
and SPE, the required consulting services could be fairly modest. EdL cur-
rently has consultants, retained under SPE, who have assisted with preparation
of the proposed project. To avoid implementation delays, the same consultants
will continue to assist with final design and procurement. EdL would also
need consulting assistance for supervising the implementation of the provin-
cial electrification components, and these would be employed under a separate
contract (para. 3.18) on a retainer basis. These proposed arrangements are
acceptable to IDA. At negotiations, EdL agreed that it would appoint a con-
sultant to assist with engineering and procurement, not later than December
31, 1992. A consultant for post-procurement project management and construc-
tion supervision is expected to be appointed not later than September 30, 1993.



- 24 -
3.16      The distribution component of SPE is expected to be completed by
end 1992. Construction of the proposed project could thus begin by mid 1993
and be completed by the end of 1996. The project's other works and services
would be completed within the same period. A table, including a tentative
schedule for procurement and the procedures to be applied, is shown in Annex
3.11. The organization of the project office is given in Annex 3.12. Annex
3.13 provides an implementation schedule for the proposed project.
3.17      Despite the Lao Government's efforts to promote the participation
of the private sector in the economy, EdL could not elicit much interest from
local private contractors in the construction of similar distribution networks
under SPE. As a result, a state company was founded under EdL's auspices in
1990 to undertake that activity. That company constructed the networks within
the cost estimates, either on or slightly ahead of schedule. Thus, to capi-
talize on its earlier investment, EdL plans that construction under the pro-
posed project of 22 kV and low voltage lines, step-down transformers, house-
wiring and connections will be undertaken by that same company. This arrange-
ment is satisfactory.
G. Procurement
3.18      About US$28 million equivalent of goods and works to be financed by
IDA under the proposed project will be procured by international competitive
bidding (ICB) in accordance with the Bank Group's Procurement Guidelines. For
the four river-crossings, estimated to cost about US$1.7 million equivalent,
EdL would select according to ICB one or more contractors to supply and in-
stall the facilities; once selected, the contractor(s) would enter into a
tripartite contract with EdL and EGAT, wherein the precise responsibility of
each utility would clearly be established (para. 3.14). IDA would review this
contract before it is finalized. Minor equipment, -testing instruments, and
tools, with a cost of less than US$100,000 per contract and an aggregate cost
of not more than US$2 million in total value, may be procured by International
Shopping (IS), in accordance with the Bank's Procurement Guidelines. Spare
parts for existing equipment, with an aggregate cost of not more than US$2
million in total value, may be procured from original suppliers through direct
contracting, according to terms and conditions acceptable to IDA. The list of
spares would need to 3e preapproved by IDA. All bidding packages financed by
IDA estimated to cost more than US$500,000 equivalent would be subject to
IDA's prior review of procurement documents. This covers about 90% of the
foreseen IDA-financed contracts. All consultants will be selected according
to the Bank Guidelines for the Use of Consultants. The technical cooperation
arrangement will be contracted directly with TNB (para. 3.9 [d]).
3.19      A margin of preference, as allowed by the Bank's Procurement Guide-
lines, will be applied in evaluating bids for (i) equipment, procured under
ICB, from domestic bidders; and (ii) works, procured under ICB, from domestic
contractors. The margin for goods will be the lower of (i) 15% of the CIF bid
price for imported goods, or (ii) the prevailing custom duties and other im-
port taxes. The margin for works will be 7Y/2.
3.20      The procurement arrangements for the proposed project are detailed
in Annex 3.11 and summarized in Table 3.3 below:
