H. Disbursements
3.21      The proposed credit would be disbursed against: (i) 100 percent of
foreign expenditures for directly imported equipment and materials quoted on
CIF basis; (ii) 100 percent of ex-factory cost for locally manufactured items
procured according to ICB; (iii) 75 percent of local expenditures for other
items procured locally; (iv) 75 percent of the value of spare parts up to a
maximum of US$2 million; and (v) 100 percent of expenditures for consulting
services and training. Full documentation would be required for all expendi-
tures for consultants' services, studies and overseas training, and all con-
tracts for goods and works valued at more than US$100,000 equivalent. Dis-
bursements for goods and works less than US$100,000 equivalent would be made
on the basis of itemized statements of expenditures (SOEs). The documentation
for SOE supported expenditures would be retained by EdL, and made available
for review by IDA supervision missions. A Special Account would be estab-
lished at Banque pour le Commerce Exterieur Lao to facilitate payment of eli-
gible small expenditures. This account would be maintained in US dollars,



- 26 -
with an authorized allocation of US$1 million. The account would be used for
both local and foreign expenditures of less than US$100,000 equivalent in all
categories. All expenditures above US$100,000 equivalent could be submitted
directly to IDA for payment. Applications for replenishment would be submit-
ted quarterly or when the amount withdrawn exceeds 50Z of the initial deposit,
whichever comes sooner. At negotiations, the Government agreed that it would
have (i) the activity in the Special Account as well as all Statements of
Expenditure maintained in support of disbursements audited annually by an
independent auditor acceptable to IDA, and (ii) furnish the report on this
audit to IDA not later than September 30 of each year. This audit could be
performed in the context of the audit of EdL's annual accounts, or separately,
as appropriate.
3.22      The proposed project includes a retroactive financing provision to
reimburse up to US$140,000 of eligible expenditures. This would be used to
refund advance payments for consultants that are retained after June 1, 1992
but prior to credit approval.
3.23      Annex 3.14 gives the disbursement schedule for the proposed Credit.
The closing date for the Credit would be June 30, 1998, which would allow
sufficient time after project commissioning for the payments of contractual
retention amounts. The disbursement schedule is based on experience obtained
during the implementation of the SPE project which is very close to the re-
gional profile for power projects. It does accelerate ahead of the profile in
the last two years so as to coincide with the expected completion of the pro-
ject. EdL has demonstrated its capacity for completing IDA-financed projects
with only minor delays; moreover, distribution network projects have short
lead times and implementation periods. Thus, the assumption of a disbursement
schedule that would end somewhat ahead of the regional profile is warranted.
L. Monitoring and Reporting
3.24      Satisfactory procedures for monitoring the progress of project exe-
cution and the loss reduction program as well as EdL's financial condition
will be arranged on the basis of those being used under SPE. Progress reports
on project execution and the loss reduction program are expected to be fur-
nished quarterly to IDA. Financial reports for the project and for EdL will
be furnished annually. IDA would also supervise implementation of the project
through field missions; a tentative summary of supervision activities and
missions is presented in Annex 3.15. EdL will furnish a project completion
report to IDA within six months of the closing date of the proposed credit.
J. Environment
3.25      The Mekong River overhead crossings and the distribution components
of the project will be designed and erected according to current technological
practices and will cause minimum disturbance to the environment. EdL has
routed the 22 kV lines along existing roads and located the substations so as
to have minimum adverse visual impact and avoid displacement of people. As of
appraisal, based on the details provided regarding the routing of planned
networks, the proposed project was deemed not to present adverse environmental
impacts; however, as the development of distribution networks is a dynamic
activity, EdL will continue reviewing the electrification components for po-



- 27 -
tentially adverse environmental effects, and will arrange for mitigatory mea-
sures as needed. At negotiations, EdL agreed that it would (i) consult with
IDA regarding any change in the content of the project that would (a) alter
the routing of planned networks, or (b) include under the project the devel-
opment of additional networks; (ii) furnish an environmental impact assessment
of the proposed change in the project where in IDA's judgement such an assess-
ment is deemed to be necessary; and (iii) incorporate IDA's comments with re-
gard to the environmental impact assessment into the design of the proposed
change.
3.26      The terms of reference for the feasibility and design of the exten-
sion to the Nam Ngum power station includes a provision for an environmental
impact assessment. That assessment would be reviewed with IDA prior to final-
ization of designs of that future project.



- 28 -
4. FINANCE
A. Introduction
4.1       During the last five years, in connection with the Government's New
Economic Mechanism, EdL has been given increasing degrees of autonomy. It now
enjoys considerable operating freedom as an enterprise, and has a fair degree
of responsibility over its operating costs that are not related to personnel.
However, EdL must follow the Government's personnel and compensation policies;
and the Government, itself, makes the final decisions concerning tariffs,
investment programs, and financial resource management.
4.2       Albeit that the exchange rate between the Kip and the US dollar has
been relatively stable during the last two years, the previous five were
marked by high inflation, currency instability and a multiplicity of exchange
rates. Moreover, a substantial portion of EdL's revenues is realized in US
dollars and most of its investment financing is raised in foreign exchange;
and EdL maintains separate US dollar and local-currency accounts. Therefore,
the financial analysis that follows has been performed in current US dollars.
For this analysis, local expenditures are converted to US dollar equivalents
at the prevailing rate of Kn 700 - US$1.
B. Past and Present Financial Perfornance
4.3       EdL's financial performance for the period 1988-91 is given in An-
nex 4.1.  Key financial indicators for the period are presented in Table 4.1.
4.4       During the past four years, EdL has performed in accordance with
the objectives agreed at negotiations of SPE, and has made substantial contri-
butions to the Government's general revenues. During all four years, its
contribution to investment from internal cash generation has exceeded 20%,
although just barely in 1990; moreover, debt service coverage has substantial-
ly exceeded the agreed 1.1 throughout the period. However, this performance
is the direct result of (i) the relative profitability of sales to Thailand,
and (ii) EdL's still low levels of debt repayment. In 1990 and 1991, EdL sold
70% of its generation to EGAT; those sales accounted for about US$18 million
in revenues (or 86% of EdL's total) in 1990, and more than US$20 million (or
82% of EdL's total) in 1991. EdL's total debt service was US$6 million, in-
cluding principal repayments of US$1.6 million, in 1990, and US$4 million,
including repayments of US$1.7 million, in 1991; however, EdL was still enjoy-
ing grace periods with regard to its substantial borrowings for Xeset and SPE.

4.5       In the future, EdL's performance should decline, as (i) profitable
exports are displaced by more costly supplies for local consumption;
(ii) hydrological conditions in the Nam Ngum catchment area are less favorable
than during the last two years; and (iii) EdL cost structure begins to reflect
the substantial burden of debt service related to projects that are either
recently completed or in advanced stages of construction:
(a)  Sales to Thalland.  During 1991, EdL and EGAT renegotiated the
pricing provisions of their power sales agreement. The new ar-
rangement (para. 4.11), which is based on time of day pricing, will
likely result in an 18X increase in average export revenues. How-
ever, for the medium-term, low revenue-high cost local sales will
inexorably substitute for more profitable exports as a result of
growing local demand. In the longer-term, EdL has plans for a num-
ber of investments that would maintain the balance between exports
and local sales (para. 1.21); however, because the Nam Ngum plant
was largely financed by grants and the loans financing the new ca-
pacity will carry a market rate, EdL's long-term additional exports
will be much less profitable than its current sales to EGAT.



- 30 -
(b) Rainfall In the Nam Ngum Catchment Area. EdL's financial health
depends on favorable rains in the Nam Ngum catchment area.  Because
rains were bountiful and water levels were high, Nam Ngum genera-
tion exceeded 800 GWh in both 1990 and 1991. EdL may export only
its residual generation after satisfying local demand. Thus, in
any given year, the energy available from Nam Ngum for export is
not likely to exceed recent levels, and could well be much less.
(c) Escalatlng Debt Servlce Reauirements. In the last two years, EdL's
debt service requirements have varied between US$4-6 million, de-
pending on whether EdL chooses to capitalize or expense interest
during construction (EdL's taxable income is determined after ex-
pensed interest; however, interest when capitalized does not simi-
larly lower taxable income). By 1994, when EdL is (i) repaying its
loans for Xeset, SPE, and the Northern Transmission Line Project,
and (ii) paying significant interest on loans for the proposed pro-
ject as well as for the Nam Song/Nam Leuk water diversions, its
debt service obligations will rise to about US$14 million per year
(assuming it capitalizes interest during construction; otherwise,
debt service will be considerably higher). By 1998, when EdL moves
to che next generation of projects and loans, debt service will
move up to about US$21 million per year.
As the terms of its export sales are fixed for the next five years, EdL will
increasingly need to cover the escalating debt service burden from domestic
revenues. By the mid 1990s, the ability of export revenues to cross-subsidize
local consumption will rapidly be eroding; by the late 1990s, the level of
domestic revenues would need to approach marginal cost parity (para. 5.20).
4.6       Despite EdL's healthy debt. service coverage of the last few years
and its high current ratio for 1988-90, liquidity has been a concern. The
Government has been withholding export taxes at source, and it claims other
remittances according to a very tight schedule. Moreover, EdL's staff and
creditors must be paid on time, while system losses and collection of revenues
have sometimes been problematic (paras. 2.15 and 4.8). Thus, despite favor-
able liquidity indicators, EdL's operating cash flow has been constrained
throughout the period. This situation must necessarily worsen in coming
years, as EdL's debt service requirements are expected to increase sharply.
This will place added pressure on local tariffs.
Valuation of Assets
4.7       Revaluation of assets is not an accepted accounting practice in the
Lao PDR. At the end of 1990, EdL's gross fixed assets had an historical value
of US$164 million. At appraisal, a pro forma revaluation of assets was under-
taken; it indicated that EdL's gross fixed assets had a replacement value of
about US$346 million as of year-end 1990.  The Nam Ngum and Xeset hydroelec-
tric plants, which are not likely to need replacement for another 40-50 years,
comprise a substantial portion of these fixed assets. Moreover, EdL has only
limited input in investment and pricing decisions, and the bulk of its sales
are subject to the terms of a long-term international agreement. Thus, asset
revaluation would have only limited usefulness to an organization like EdL.



- 31 -
Accounts Receivable
4.8       EdL has a history of slow collection of revenues for sales within
the Lao PDR. At appraisal of SPE, accounts receivable turnover for local
sales was averaging about 150 days, net of unread meter accruals41 of about
25 days; however, a large number of residential consumers and Government agen-
cies had more than one year's receivables outstanding. In connection with
SPE, EdL agreed to implement an action program aimed at reducing receivables
for local sales to less than 90 days (net of unread meter accruals) by 1991.
As of late 1991, EdL's accounts receivable for local sales were still about
150 days, net of unread meter accruals. However, EdL has shown substantial
improvement since early 1991, when it introduced a new computerized billing
system; and it expects that local receivables will drop to about 100-110 days
within the next six months, once it begins remitting bills to local consumers
monthly instead of every two to three months as is current practice. Although
EdL's receivables for its export sp'.es are running about 85 days, these are
currently based on the terms of the export sales agreement with EGAT.
4.9       Under the Government's former code of accounts, EdL was prohibited
from writing off receivables, even if they were deemed uncollectible; however,
write-offs are allowed under the new code, provided they are authorized by the
Government. The Government could hold EdL more accountable for its commercial
performance by (i) removing from EdL's books receivables that are clearly
uncollectible, and (ii) monitoring EdL's performance in collecting bills that
have acknowledged value. For this reason, the Government should authorize EdL
to (i) screen its receivables and identify those that are of dubious value;
and (ii) write-off those that are old and for which prospects of collection
are poor; and (iii) establish an annual provision for bad debts. As a first
step, EdL was asked to evaluate and dispose of some Kn 94 million in accounts
receivable that have been outstanding since before January 1, 1988. As of
December 31, 1991, the Government and EdL decided that about Kn 39 million of
that amount was uncollectible and therefore was to be written off against
EdL's 1991 accounts. EdL succeeded in collecting some Kn 31 million, and
would continue its active efforts to collect the remaining Kn 24 million dur-
ing 1992. Whatever would remain uncollected by the end of the year would be
written off against EdL's 1992 accounts. This is satisfactory.
4.10      In conducting the analysis related to collection of these receiv-
ables, EdL recognized that it has an important problem of slow collections
from Government offices, and from provincial and local units that take elec-
tricity for irrigation. Some such consumers have chronic arrears of a year or
substantially more. An acceptable tentative program (Annex 4.2), which would
settle these arrears over a two year period through budgetary and tax offsets,
was discussed with the Government and EdL in March 1992. The Government and
EdL would need to exchange letters to put that program into effect. At nego-
tiations, the Government agreed that it will implement a satisfactory program
to reduce the arrears to EdL of Government, provincial and local units, ac-
cording to a timetable acceptable to IDA.
ij/ EdL records as sales, energy that has been delivered through the distri-
bution system but not yet billed. Since meters are intended to be read
in the first week of the month, this accrual averages about 25 days
(from the date of the reading until the end of the month).



- 32 -
C. Tariffs
4.11      EdL's only sales at high voltage (115 kV) are exports to Thailand.
Beginning October 1, 1991, the pricing provision of the sales agreement for
Nam Ngum electricity has been based on alternative cost (para. 5.15); this
tariff includes (i) a rate of about US5.8/kWh for the evening peak (6:30 p.m.
to 9:30 p.m.); (ii) a rate of about US3.3/kWh for the daytime shoulder (be-
tween 7:30 a.m. and 6:30 p.m.); and (iii) an off-peak rate of about US2.6/kWh
applicable to the remaining nighttime hours. This arrangement is valid for
twelve years; however, the constituents of alternative cost will be reviewed
and adjusted every four years. This arrangement is likely to yield average
revenues of US3.6/kWh, an increase of about 18X from the US3.05/kWh that had
been in effect during 1986-91. The arrangement governing exports from Xeset
calls for EdL to realize US2.9/kWh for all sales, regardless of time of day;
that arrangement is valid until 1994, when EdL expects it to be replaced with
time of day pricir.g based on the Nam Ngum agreement. Imports into Savannakhet
and Khammouane are based on the Nam Ngum price plus a wheeling charge of
US0.5/kWh; imports into the Xeset grid are based on the Xeset price plus a
similar wheeling charge of US0.5/kWh.
4.12      Until July 1992, the local rate structure, which included numerous
tariffs applicable to different user categories and a lifeline block of 200
kWh/month applicable to virtually all user categories, produces average reve-
nues (i) well below export revenues in the Northern System, which receives its
supplies from Nam Ngum; (ii) slightly below export revenues in the Southern
System, which now receives most of its supplies from Xeset; and (iii) substan-
tially below the import price in Central System, which imports its supplies
from Thailand.
(a) Northern System. As a result of a July 1991 tariff increase, aver-
age revenues for residential consumption in the Vientiane area rose
to USl.6/kWh from about USl.l/kWh under the previous tariff; sim-
ilarly, average revenues for non-residential consumption increased
to US2.40/kWh from the previous level of about USl.7/kWh.
(b) Southern Svstem. Until June 1991, most of the energy consumed in
this area was imported from Thailand by EdL at about US3.55C/kWh,
and retailed to consumers by provincial utilities. These utilities
realized an average of about Kn 19/kWh (about US2.7/kWh since
1989) and remitted Kn 8/kWh (about USl.l/kWh) to EdL. When EdL
acquired these utilities in June 1991, it began supplying the area
mainly from Xeset. EdL has retained the retail tariffs. Although
Xeset supplies cost less to produce than the retail price, the mar-
gin is not enough to cover the cost of the distribution system.
(c) Central Sgstem. Until June 1991, the provincial utilities serving
these areas received their supplies of imported electricity from
EdL under arrangements similar to those in effect in the Southern
System. Here too, the prevailing retail tariff yielded average
revenues of Kn 19/kWh (US2.7/kWh). After EdL acquired these util-
ities, it retained their retail tariffs, albeit that the import
price increased in October 1991 to an average of US4.1/kWh.
4.13      At the end of 1991, export sales represented more than 80% of EdL's
revenues. With the export tariff for the Nam Ngum grid being fixed for four



- 33 -
years and adjustments thereafter expected to be modest, revenues from local
sales would need to shoulder the major burden of increases in EdL's cash re-
quirements (especially debt service) projected for the next ten years. The
Government was aware that local tariffs were insufficient to cover the cost of
supply. While the Government wished to continue cross-subsidizing local con-
sumption from export sales, the capacity for cross-subsidization was eroded as
the cost of exports and the proportion of local sales were projected to in-
crease progressively.
4.14      In July 1992, a national tariff was established for grid supplied
electricity. The lifeline block was eliminated for all but residential con-
sumers; under the new tariff, commercial consumers will be charged Kn 47/kWh
and industrial and government consumers Kn 30/kWh on their entire consumption.
A three block structure now applies for residential consumers: (i) their first
100 kWh per month will be charged at the lifeline rate of Kn 8/kWh, (ii) their
second 100 kWh per month will be charged at the subsidized rate of Kn 15/kWh,
and (iii) all their remaining consumption will be charged at Kn 25/kWh. Irri-
gation consumption will now be charged at the subsidized rate of Kn 7/kWh.
Average revenue from local consumption is expected to be about Kn 26/kWh.
This is more than the cost of providing electricity to each grid, and is
enough to cover a significant proportion of distribution and commercial costs.
The level of these new tariffs would need to be protected against adverse
fluctuations in the rate of exchange. EdL's continued compliance with its
agreement under SPE to meet 20Z of its average annual investment progrem from
internal cash generation presumas that it would maintain the level of its
local charges relative to the US dollar.
D. Financing Plan
4.15      EdL's financing plan for the period of project implementation,
1992-97, is given in Table 4.2.
4.16      The proposed project, including interest during construction, rep-
resents about 22X of EdL's investment program for the 1992-97 period. The
proposed credit is expected to meet 18X of EdL's total financing requirement
for the period. At negotiations, the Government agreed to onlend the proceeds
of the proposed credit to EdL for a term of 20 years (including five years of
grace on repayment of principal) at an interest rate of 8.0%. EdL will bear
the foreign exchange risk. The signing of a Subsidiary Loan Agreement with
terms and conditions satisfactory to IDA would be a condition of effectiveness
of the proposed credit. Anozher 31 of EdL's financing requirements for the
period will be financed by IDA's Credit 1826-LA (SPE).
4.17      During the project period, EdL expects to meet about 21X of its
overall investment needs from internal cash generation. About 57% of EdL's
investment requirements are expected to be met from other loans from official
lenders. During this period, Government financing of EdL's investment program
is projected at less than US$1 million (less than 1X of EdL's aggregate fi-
nancing requirements). While EdL has the capacity to absorb the additional
debt expected to be raised during the period, it must manage its debt and its
foreign exchange exposure carefully. The debt to total capital ratio (net of
any reserve for the revaluation of assets) was 501 at the end of 1990; it is
expected to rise to approximately 661 by 1995 and remain at that level through
the year 2000. While this level of debt is still acceptable albeit on the



- 34 -
high side, at negotiations an understanding was reached with the Government
that it would consider making additional equity investments should EdL's debt
rise above 70X of its total capital.

4.19       To assure EdL's financial health, at negotiations, EdL agreed that
it will take steps, including but not limited to proposing increases to its
tariff and implementing approved tariff adjustments, to cover its operating
costs (excluding depreciation), net increases in working capital, major repa-
irs expencdtures, debt service, and government tax obligations, and meet 20X
of its average annual investment program from internal cash generation. EdL
also agreed that it will refrain from incurring additional debt unless a rea-
sonable forecast of revenues net of cash operating expenses, increases in wor-
king capital, major repairs expenditures, dividends, and Government tax obli-
gations are sufficient to meet 1.1 times its aggregate debt service obliga-
tions for each year during the term of the debt to be incurred. In addition,
the Government agreed that it will take whatever steps are necessary, includ-
ing but not limited to adjusting electricity rates, to enable EdL to meet its
self-financing and debt service coverage targets. These are the same finan-
cial performance targets that were agreed under SPE. The financial projec-
tions tested these targets and found that they still yield acceptable finan-
cial performance while also indicating tariffs that increase smoothly to need-
ed levels over time.



- 36 -
4.20      Based on these financial performance targets, EdL is expected to
realize acceptable, and relatively consistent financial performance throughout
the projection period. The rate of return on historically valued assets is
expected to hold at current levels of about 6X; its rate of return on notion-
ally revalued assets is actually expected to increase from about 3X to 41, as
new assets contribute an increasing share of gross fixed assets. Although the
self-financing ratio is expected to average nearly 301 throughout the period,
that ratio is somewhat misleading. Every quarter, the Government withdraws
equity equal to about 461 of depreciation expenses; after these withdrawals,
EdL will only be self-financing a still satisfactory average of about 21X of
its investment program every year. The Government should also benefit from
EdL's complying with these targets; during the projection period, remittances
of taxes are expected to triple from current levels of US$4 million to about
US$12 million in 2000.
4.21      Between now and 2000, EdL has planned an aggressive investment pro-
gram, valued at about US$268 million (including physical and price contingen-
cies). About (i) US$153 million is to increase the amount of generation in
the Nam Ngum grid; (ii) US$34 Lillion is for transmission lines to intercon-
nect the Nam Ngum grid with Luang Prabang and the remote northern provinces;
(iii) US$47 million is for SPE and the proposed project; and (iv) US$35 mil-
lion is for other distribution and miscellaneous activities. This program
appears optimistic, both in terms of EdL's implementation capacity and the
Government's resource constraints. While export sales should provide EdL with
enough revenues to recover the cost of the generation investments, their mar-
gins are not sufficient to cross-subsidize the recovery of the costs of the
transmission, distribution, and miscellaneous investments. The tariff levels
indicated by the recommended financial performance targets should be enough to
enable EdL to recover the cost of these investments from local sales revenues.

5. ECONOMIC JUSTIFICATION
A. Electricity Demand
5.1       EdL's strategy for developing transmission and distribution calls
for prudent expansion of grid supplies to areas with proven demand for elec-
tricity as a result of existing economic activity that has been driven by
alternative energy sources. To determine the priority areas for expansion
under the proposed project, a set of potential transmission and distribution
projects was screened for economic feasibility, based on expected residential
and productive demand (para. 3.2). Field surveys established realistic demand
estimates that were reduced from previous plan projections. Only firmly com-
mitted productive demand and conversion of existing facilities were taken into
account. The consultants' report with the results of the field surveys is
available in the project file. The demand projections for selected areas
covered by the proposed project are summarized in Annex 5.1.
5.2       The areas with high demand potential that were selected for
detailed analysis after screening include:
(a) Areas close to the Xeset-based Southern Grid, including portions of
Champassak, Saravane, and Sekong provinces;
(b) Areas in the vicinity of Savannakhet town;
(c) Areas in Khammouane province close to the town of Thakhek, and in
the Se Bang Fai plain; and
(d) The town and vicinity of Paksane in Bolikhamxay province.
5.3       A Southern Grid is being created through the electrification in-
vestments under SPE and the ADB-financed Xeset project. From 1991 onwards,
electricity consumption in the new grid is expected to grow from the previous-
ly supply-constrained 8 GWh per year to about 28 GWh in 1995, and about 46 GWh
by the year 2000. Based on income and population growth, consumption in ex-
isting distribution networks is projected to grow at about 7% per year. In
addition, the extension of lines to areas where replacement of alternative
fossil fuel use is likely, will result in initial consumption growth rates in
those areas of about 25-30% per annum. Although residential consumption is
expected to grow more slowly than productive uses of electricity in the early
stages, the share taken by residential users is gradually expected to increase
to about 50% of consumption as these systems mature.
5.4       A supply-constrained situation (similar to that in the South before
electrification and Xeset) also exists in Paksane (Bolikhamxay province) and
the Se Bang Fai Rlain (Khammouane province). The availability of incremental
electricity supply in these areas will result in an initial jump in connec-
tions and consumption, followed by a tapering of the consumption growth rate
to about 5% per annum. While the consumption pattern in Paksane, a thriving
town on the Mekong, is dominated by industrial and other productive users who
account for about 75% of consumption, the more rural Se Bang Fai plain is ex-
pected to show a consumption split similar to the Southern provinces, with
about half the consumption being taken by residential consumers.



- 38 -
5.5       In contrast, the towns of Thakhe  and Savannakhet are already con-
nected to the Thai grid, and consumption there has been growing without a
supply constraint; in fact, the main constraint in these areas is the lack of
funding for extending local distribution networks. During the 1980s, elec-
tricity consumption in these two towns and surrounding areas has been growing
at about 151 per annum. During the 1990s, this rapid early growth in the
existing network is expected to decrease to about 7X per annum, but will be
supplemented by new connections as these areas are added to the local grid.
B. Least-Cost Supply
5.6       The high-potential demand centers to be served by project compo-
nents through the creation or extension of provincial grids are supposed to be
supplied from the lowest-cost source of generation, and in the most cost-ef-
fective way possible. The relatively low demand volumes as well as the dis-
persed nature of rural demand require that the network design and the choice
of the supply source be selected conservatively.
5.7       The least-cost supply source in the Southern Provinces, now linked
to the Thai grid by the Xeset-related transmission line, is a combination of
Xeset and Selabam hydro supply, supplemented by dry-season imports from Thai-
land. Isolated mini-hydro schemes appear excessively expensive, and small
part-time diesel supply may be preferable for remote areas until their econom-
ic development justifies a grid connection. The selected design calls for
moderate extensions of the existing grid in Champassak and Saravane provinces,
and an extension into part of Sekong province. Further extensions to the load
centers in the remainder of Sekong province, and to Attopeu province, do not
yet appear to be economically justified.
5.8       For demand centers in all other selected provinces, the lowest-cost
source of supply is the Thai grid via 22 kV transmission links. For the es-
tablished Mekong River crossings in Thakhek and Savannakhet, the most effi-
cient medium-term solution for strengthening the capacity of those linkages is
replacement of the existing underwater cables with overhead line crossings.
For supplying the Paksane and Se Bang Fai areas, new overhead river crossings
are to be established, taking advantage of nearby Thai system substations with
adequate capacity. The alternatives of supplying Paksane via a transmission
line from the Nam Ngum grid, and supplying Se Bang Fai via a transmission link
from Savannakhet and its corresponding Thai substation were rejected because
of the cost advantages to be realized by using a river crossing. In the case
of Savannakhet, the least-cost solution would call for a new 12 kV double-
circuit river crossing, to be upgraded to 115 kV at a later date. This option
is not available because of EGAT's preference for an immediate move to 115 kV
to accommodate its voltage constraints.
C. Economic Rate of Return
5.9       Having determined the least-cost nature of supply sources and net-
work configuration for the five separate project areas, each component was
subjected to economic cost/benefit analysis to determine whether the invest-
ment is justified at this time. Costs and benefits were expressed in terms of
CIF or equivalent border prices, and estimated entirely in terms of US$. Fol-
lowing the opening of the economy to market forces during recent years, the



- 39 -
local cost component reflects market prices relatively closely, and the Kn/$
exchange rate of about 700 is an equilibrium rate acceptable for analysis.
The costs of alternative fuels in local markets now reflect the economic cost
of delivering them to the consumers. In the case of project areas with river
crossing investments, the full cost of the link to the Thai grid (including
the costa borne by EGAT) are incorporated into the project cost.
5.10      The economic benefits of the individual projects consist of con-
sumers' observed willingness to pay for energy. Consumers' valuation of ener-
gy supply is calculated according to a demand function that (i) includes the
observed willingness to pay for alternative energy sources such as kerosene
for lighting, or diesel for small engines and pumps, and (ii) declines to the
observed cost of using electricity, based on the tariff and associated costs
(Annex 5.2). The agreed export and import prices between Thailand and Lao PDR
form the basis for the valuation of the cost of bulk supply to the project
areas (para. 4.11). The peak-period export tariff of US5.8C/kWh and the
shoulder-period tariff of US3.3C/kWh, appropriately weighted to account for
the consumption pattern in the project areas, results in a medium to long-term
opportunity cost of exports foregone of US3.90/kWh in the Southern Grid.
Similarly, the adding of the agreed wheeling charge of US0.5C/kWh results in a
bulk supply cost of US4.4C/kWh for the other project areas that receive bulk
supply from the Thai grid only.
5.11      The results of the cost/benefit analysis (Annex 5.3) show a base-
case economic internal rate of return (IRR) in the range of 10-21X (the range
would be 17-21X if the proposed project had not had to include the second best
solution for Savannakhet to accommodate EGAT's voltage constraints). In all
five selected project areas, the proposed distribution network investment
(tincluding the strengthening of existing and establishment of new river cross-
ing links) is an economically feasible solution to relieve power supply bot-
tlenecks, or replace higher-cost energy supply. An increase in capital cost
of 15-30X, based on unexpectedly higher river crossing costs or local network
configuration changes, still would result in an IRR of 7-18X. An unexpectedly
poor demand development of 30X less consumption than projected, would result
in an IRR range of 7-15X (Annex 5.3).
5.12      Total benefits from the proposed area electrification projects are
likely to be higher than those quantified; in the projections of electricity
consumption, only replacement of existing alternatives and firmly committed
incremental productive use were considered. If other potential small commer-
cial and industrial demand, now suppressed because of the high cost or lack of
availability of alternatives, were considered, the computed IRR would rise.
Similarly, the benefits of increased convenience of electric lighting have not
been taken into account. Evidence from areas electrified under previous Lao
projects suggests that penetration of connections is quite fast, and often
exceeds the base-case projections in the areas selected under the project.
D. Benefits and Risks
5.13      Benefits.  The main benefit of the proposed project is that the
provincial electrification component will extend the availability of grid
supplied electricity to areas in the country's central and southern regions
that are already experiencing economic growth, and thereby result in energy
cost savings in those areas. Also, the system reliability and institutional



- 40 -
development components should result in (i) improving the reliability of sup-
plies to consumers throughout EdL's networks; (ii) increasing the available
technical competence at all levels of EdL; and (iii) transferring to EdL new
techniques for managing a widely dispersed technical enterprise. Women would
benefit from the improved quality of life in the home, and ftom an ernanced
capacity to do productive work in the home, such as sewing, weaving, and other
piecework.
5.14      Rlsks.  The proposed project faces no major risks.  It involves
EdL's use of standard technologies to develop systems with which it has con-
siderable experience. The minor risks include: (i) Possible minor construc-
tion delays if EGAT does not complete its portion of the interconnecting links
on time. This risk would not affect the IRR significantly. To reduce this
risk, EdL and EGAT have already held detailed communications with regard to
designs and schedules. (ii) Constraints in the supply of cement resulting
from the building boom in Thailand could lead to construction delays or a
higher cost of poles if cement is imported from afar. To mitigate this risk,
EdL is advancing procurement, and the physical contingencies provide cushion
if EdL must use cement imported from afar. (iii) Any prolonged drought would
have a serious impact on EdL's exports and its capability to mobilize coun-
terpart funds. However, since the bulk of EdL's counterpart investment in the
proposed project is for transfer payments, this risk should not affect project
implementation unduly.
E. Tariffs and Cost of Supply
5.15      All electricity consumption in the Lao PDR is intimately linked to
exports to or imports from the Thai grid. Thus, electricity in the Lao con-
text is a traded commodity with an agreed border price that determines oppor-
tunity cost at the margin. Any incremental consumption within the Lao PDR in
any of the separate local grids directly implies a marginal loss of export
earnings, or an incremental import expenditure. Thus, marginal cost in gener-
al equals the agreed price between EGAT and EdL at the border substation at
the appropriate transfer voltage. The recently revised export/import agree-
ment between the two countries calls for a time-of-day bulk export tariff to
Thailand of US5.8C/kWh during peak periods, US3.3C/kWh during shoulder daytime
periods, and US2.60/kWh for nighttime off-peak periods. At the import points
into Lao grids, a wheeling charge of US0.50/kWh is added to these prices.
5.16      If weighted as direct export revenue, the new tariff results in an
average revenue of about US3.6/kWh. For purposes of determining the opportu-
nity cost of domestic incremental consumption, the tariff structure is weight-
ed by the typical pattern of local consumption, and yields an average cost of
US3.90/kWh at high voltage, and US4.3C/kWh delivered to the low-voltage con-
sumer after distribution losses. In Lao grids that import from the Thai grid,
the addition of the wheeling charge results in a retail-level average cost of
supply of US4.8M/kWh. The incremental cost of providing and maintair.ing the
distribution network has to be added to these costs to arrive at the full cost
of power supplied to Lao consumers.
5.17      The marginal cost of the distribution network in the Vientiane Area
has been estimated in an ADB-financed tariff study at about US$70/kW/year on a
lifetime basis for the equipment installed. If applied to an industrial/com-
mercial consumer with about a 50% load factor, the total energy and capacity



- 41 -
cost of supplying this consumer is about US6/kWh. On the other hand, a resi-
dential consumer with a higher share of peak-period consumption and a low load
factor of 30X incurs a total cost of close to US80/kWh. By comparison, aver-
age tariffs for residential consumers in Vientiane in 1991 were USl.6C/kWh,
and those for non-residential consumers US2.4/kWh.
5.18      Consumers in the existing networks in other provinces impose mar-
ginal costs on the supplying utility similar to those computed for the Vien-
tiane area, except that costs in grids that import from Thailand at the margin
include a cost that is USO.5/kWh higher than in Vientiane and the Southern
Grid. New consumers in networks to be established under the RroRosed proJect
naturally impose significantly higher marginal costs of supply on the system,
as they have to bear the incremental cost of distribution and bulk supply
infrastructure and the strengthening of river-crossing transmission. Depend-
ing on the extent of incremental investment necessary, the marginal costs of
electricity delivered to consumers vary considerably in the separate local
grids: non-residential consumers typically impose a marginal cost in the range
of US7-120/kWh, while residential consumers cost the system in the range of
US10-18C/kWh. By comparison, until the new tariff was introduced in July
1992, electricity rates outside Vientiane (without distinction between consum-
er categories) averaged about US2.7C/kWh.
5.19      Clearly, local tariff levels are still below marginal cost esti-
mates, and insufficient to support the network expansion, or to provide a
satisfactory financial result for EdL beyond the next few years (para. 4.13).
Although the marginal cost of electricity supply lies below the economic cost
of consumers' alternatives, thus making the project investments economically
attractive, EdL would not be able to continue network expansion without recov-
ering a larger share of the marginal cost and of the rent now accruing to
local consumers. In addition, three tariff structure issues are important:
(a)  In view of the significant marginal cost differences among individ-
ual local grids, it would be desirable to have different cost-re-
lated tariffs in each grid. However, socio-political and national
equity considerations may require that tariff differentials within
Lao PDR be kept at a more modest level, even in the long run.
(b)  Current tariff levels for residential consumers in Vientiane are
below those for non-residential (industrial and commercial) ones,
although marginal cost estimates would indicate that the relation-
ship should be reversed. A gradual restructuring is necessary to
eliminate this distortion in the tariff structure in Vientiane, and
to avoid similar distortions in other provinces in the future.
(c)  With their first 100 kWh per month being charged at a lifeline rate
and their second 100 kWh per month at a subsidized intermediate
rate, electricity is still very inexpensive to residential consum-
ers; based on current patterns, 86X of residential consumers would
have their full electricity requirement fall within the subsidized
blocks. The amounts of electricity covered by the subsidized
blocks needs to be reduced significantly.
5.20      The proposed project addresses both the tariff level and the tariff
structure issues. A continued application of the financial covenants agreed
to under SPE (para. 4.19) would gradually move tariff levels closer to margin-



- 42 -
al cost estimates over the next six to ten years. Compliance with those fi-
nancial covenants would increase average revenues in all Lao grids from the
recently increased level of US3.70/kWh to about US50/kWh in the mid-1990s, and
beyond US70/kWh by 1997. On a weighted basis, this would come close to achiev-
ing marginal cost parity for the bulk of consumers in the system. In addi-
tion, over time, EdL needs to address the tariff structure issues. The tariff
study (para. 5.17) Inter alls addressed these issues. As EdL has the requi-
site expertise, no further consulting studies of tariffs are needed; however,
the Government and EdL need specifically to formulate a policy on supply cost
differentials and the size of the lifeline consumption block. At negotia-
tions, EdL agreed that it would (i) by June 30, 1994, communicate to IDA its
policy on tariff structure as well as measures to address the issues of supply
cost differentials and the size of the lifeline block; (ii) by December 31,
1994, discuss this policy and the proposed measures with the Government and
IDA; and (iii) according to a schedule acceptable to IDA, implement the mutu-
ally acceptable conclusions from those discussions.



- 43 -
6. AGREEMENTS AND RECOMMENDATION
A. Agreements
6.1       At negotiations, the Lao Government agreed that it would:
(a) Arrange for EdL to acquire the assets, liabilities and businesses
of the provincial electricity authorities serving consumers in
Bolikhamxay and Sekong provinces, prior to construction of the net-
works being financed under the proposed project (para. 2.5).
(b) Onlend the proceeds of the proposed credit to EdL for a term of 20
years (including five years of grace on repayment of principal) at
an interest rate of 8.0X (para. 4.16).
(c) Implement a satisfactory program to reduce the arrears to EdL of
Government, provincial and local units, according to a timetable
acceptable to IDA (para. 4.10).
(d) Have (i) the activity in the Special Account as well as all State-
ments of Expenditure, maintained in support of disbursements,
audited annually by an independent auditor acceptable to IDA, and
(ii) furnish the report on this audit to IDA not later than Septem-
ber 30 of each year (para. 3.21).
(e) Take whatever steps are necessary, including but not limited to
adjusting electricity rates, to enable EdL to meet its self-financ-
ing and debt service coverage targets (para. 4.19).
6.2       At negotiations, EdL agreed that it would:
(a) Furnish audited financial statements to IDA within nine months of
the end of each financial year (para. 2.21).
(b) Implement an action program to (i) monitor and reduce system los-
ses, (ii) increase its collection efficiency, and (iii) improve its
implementation of the recently computerized billing system, espe-
cially in its provincial service areas (para. 3.7).
(c) (i) By December 31, 1992, retain consultants, according to terms of
reference and following a selection procedure acceptable to IDA, to
provide technical assistance with regard to finance and accounting;
(ii) by September 30, 1993, retain consultants, according to terms
of reference and following a selection procedure acceptable to IDA,
to perform a management and organization study; (iii) by December
31, 1994, discuss with IDA the principal conclusions of these con-
sultancies; and (iv) according to a schedule acceptable to IDA, im-
plement those of the consultants' recommendations that are accept-
able to the Government, EdL and IDA (para. 3.10).
(d) Conclude a satisfactory technical cooperation arrangement with an
acceptable electric utility from the region (para. 3.10).



- 44 -
(e)  (i) By September 30, 1993, retain consult   ; according to terms of
reference and following a selection proceoure acceptable to IDA, to
conduct a feasibility and design study for _xtending the Nam Ngum
power plant; and (ii) by December 31, 1994, discuss the findings of
the study with IDA (para. 3.11).
(f) EdL would enter into an acceptable contractual arrangement with
EGAT as a condition of disbursement for the central region's net-
works (para. 3.14).
(g) Appoint a consultant to assist with engineering and procurement,
not later than December 31, 1992. (para. 3.15).
(h) (i) Consult with IDA regarding any change in the content of the
project that would (a) alter the routing of planned networks, or
(b) include under the project the development of additional net-
works; (ii) furnish an environmental impact assessment of the pro-
posed change in the project where in IDA's judgement such an as-
sessment is deemed to be necessary; and (iii) incorporate IDA's
comments with regard to the environmental impact assessment into
the design of the proposed change (para. 3.25).
(i) Take steps, including but not limited to proposing increases to its
tariff and implementing approved tariff adjustments, to cover its
operating costs (excluding depreciation), net increases in working
capital, major repairs expenditures, debt service, and government
tax obligations, and meet 20% of its average annual investment pro-
graL from internal cash generation (para 4.19).
(j)  Refrain from incurring additional debt unless a reasonable forecast
of revenues net of cash operating expenses, increases in working
capital, major repairs expenditures, dividends, and Government tax
obligations are sufficient to meet 1.1 times its aggregate debt
service obligations for each year during the term of the debt to be
incurred (para. 4.19).
(k) (i) By June 30, 1994, communicate to IDA its policy on tariff
structure as well as measures to address the issues of supply cost
differentials and the size of the lifeline block; (ii) by December
31, 1994, discuss this pclicy and the proposed measures with the
Government and IDA; and (iii) according to a schedule acceptable to
IDA, implement the mutually acceptable conclusions from those dis-
cussions (para. 5.20).
6.3       At negotiations, an understanding was reached with the Lao Govern-
ment that it would consider making additional equity investments should EdL's
debt rise above 70% of its total capital (para. 4.17).
6.4       The signing of a Subsidiary Loan Agreement, with terms and condi-
tions satisfactory to IDA, would be a condition of effectiveness of the pro-
posed credit (para. 4.16).



- 45 -
B. Recommendation
6.5       The aforementioned conditions, agreements, and understandings hav-
ing been reached, the proposed project would constitute a suitable basis for
an IDA credit of SDR 25.3 million (US$36 million equivalent) to the Lao
People's Democratic Republic.

LAO PDR
PROVINCAL GRID INTEGRATION PROJECT
Progra  for Los Reducdon
Introducdon
1.          Under the SPE projectl/, EdL has.taken the flrst stop to reduce
distribution losses, both technlcal and non-technlcal, in the jurisdictLons
that were under its control. These steps included: (i) the establlshment of
a Technical Inspection Cell for meter lnspection; (il) an increase of the
moter readlng staff; (ili) the formulation and implementation of a program for
meter testlng; (lv) the installation of a meter testLng laboratory ln
Vientiane; and (v) the introductlon of a computerLzed billlng system. In
addltion, computerlzed monitoring reports are publlshed monthly that include
informatLon for the Vientlane grld regarding load dLapatch and losses, and
progress made in implementing the program for meter testlng and replacement.
Action Plan
2.          The Action Plan that started under the SPE will be p.zrsued and
extended to provlncial authorities lncorporated in EdL. In this regard, an
Inspection Cell and a meter testlng laboratory will be established in each
provinclal entity. The program for reducing accounts recelvable will also be
strengthened with regard to customers in Vlentiane provlnce and expanded to
those in the provincial entities.
Elements of the Action Phl
3.          In addition to the continuation of actions already undertaken in
the ongoing loss reduction program, the Action Plan will include actions
listed in the Attachment to the Annex.
4.          In order to undertake the Plan, EdL will collect statistical data
on heavily loaded load centers and distribution transformers to study the
loading pattern which will pinpoint areas requiring modifications to reduce
losses. After this, the necessary steps will be taken, such as balancing load
on all three phases, reconductoring of substransmission lines and distribution
feeders, replacement of either very lightly loaded transformers with smaller
rated ones to reduce no load losses, or overloaded transformers with either
larger rated ones or additional transformers.
5.          EdL will, in particular, review connection/reconnection policies
and operational practices, penalties for illegal connections, analysis of
proposals for improvements/reorganization of these functions, and implement
penalties as a credible cure for irregularities. More specIfically:
J/   SAR No 6733-LA, Annexes 7 and S.



- 60 -
Page 2 of 5
(i)   power factor restrictions will be introduced on large consumers;
they will be required to lmprove their power factor to a 0.95
level either with their own provisions or incorporation of the
same by EdL at their expense; and
(ii) all future high voltage (HV) consumers would be required to
provide metering at HV levels to ensure that the cost of
transformer losses is to consumers account. Alternatively, EdL is
contemplating a minimum additional charge to HV consumers to
compensate for transformer losses.
6.          With regard to accounts receivable, the Plan will include measures
that increase EdL's effectiveness at collecting payments for current
consumption and arrears. Such measures could include a reinforcement in the
Vientiane province and an introduction in the provincial entities, and/or a
stricter application of EdL policy on overdue accounts, measures aiming at
making it easier for customers to pay their bills, and improved procedures for
billing and monitoring overdue accounts. In this regard, the computerized
billing system implemented in Vientiane under the SPE project will be
strengthened by providing further training to the staff, and introduced in the
provitcial entities, in a simpler version if feasible.  Measures to reduce
arrears may also include, when justified, agreements with customers on arrears
settlement schedules.
QuaUty of Services
7.          EdL distribution networks are rather new and, therefore, the
quality of services (reliability of supply, voltage levels) is rather good.
However, at this stage, EdL will start monitoring the quality of services by
recording interruptions at the consumer level (scheduled and accidental
outages) on a quarterly basis, as well as voltage drops below acceptable
levels. This will enable EdL to establish and apply the systems and
procedures that will maintain, or permit improvements in, service reliability.
These measures are related to network rehabilitation, that will be translated
in future investment program, proper network design, and improved operation
and maintenance practices.
Reporing
8.          Every six months, EdL management will prepare, for review with the
Association, a concise computerized report that will contain for each
distribution network:
(i)   a summary of load dispatched and losses (broken down in
generation, transmission, and distribution) for each month; their
cumulative value for the current year; and comparison with the
results of the previous year;
(ii) progress made in the implementation of the Action Plan with
reference to annual and/or longer-term targets.

PROVINCIAL GRED INEEGRATION PROJECr
Terms of Reference for Finance, Acounting and Computerization Consultancy
Background
1.        The Finance, Accounting and Budget component of the
Institutional Development Program under the SPE Project (IDA Credit 1826-
LA) was completed in March 1991. The gains accruing to EdL from this
program are as follows:
(a) For the first time, accounting practices and procedures were
codified through the preparation of operation manuals covering
financial accounting, project accounting, budgets and financial
forecasts.
(b) A comprehensive training program for operating and management
staff at Vientiane and the provinces was completed.
(c) The Finance Manager, Deputy Finance Manager and the Chief
Accountant have further benefitted by a six week practical
training course in power utilities at Bombay.
Proposed Terms of Reference
2.        In order to consolidate the gains of this program and to carry
it to its logical conclusion, EdL should seek further consultancy services
to cover the following:
(a) Implementation of the recommendations of the Institutional
Development Study Report.
(b) Maintenance of accounting records in accordance with the
manuals.
(c) Preparation and presentation of annual financial statements and
reports to international standards.
(d) Initiate action for the establishment of an internal audit
department.
(e) Development of a system for computerizing the operations in the
provinces in the following areas, with a view to facilitate
integration of provincial utilities through uniform practices
and procedures:



- 66 -
ANNEX 3.5
Page 2 of 4
(i)    Accounting
(il)    Billing
(iii)    Stores
(iv)    Maintenance of Statistics
(v)    Management Information Systems
The Consultant shall develop necessary software for this purpose
and advise EdL on procurement of necessary hardware.
(f)  Adequate training for EdL staff to operate the system in (e)
above.
(g)  Review of the status and operational constraints of the
computerized billing system presently operating at Vientiane and
advise EdL of further improvements to realize the full potential
of the system.
Objectie
3.        The main objective of the consul:ancy is to assist EdL in the
preparation of annual accounts and financial reports and their
presentation to Government and lending institutions at international
standards acceptable for audit by independent external auditors. To meet
this objective, it would be necessary for the consultant to assist EdL in
implementing the recommendations and procedures laid down In the
Institutional Development Report and Manuals.
Scope of Work
4.        The Consultant will advlse and assist the finance managers at
Vientiane and provincial offices in regard to the vear-end work on
preparation and completion of consolidated accounts of EdL. For this
purpose, he will:
(a)  Review the instructions to be issued by the Finance Manager,
Vientiane and the adequacy of the returns required from the
provincial offices for consolidation and presentation.
(b)  In consultation with the Finance Manager, finalize the formats
of the various schedules and work-sheets required to support the
preparation of final accounts.
(c)  As a preparatory exercise to closing of accounts, analyze the
key accounts and record the adjusting entries, where necessary,
to reflect the correct position.



- 67 -
ANNEX 3.5
Page 3 of 4
(d) Jointly with the Finance Manager, monLtor the progress of work;
and the Finance Manager will take all necessary actions to
adhere to time schedules at various stages of completlon.
(e) Assist and guide the Finance Manager to conduct a detailed
scrutiny of the draft income statement, balance sheet and
supporting schedules to ensure that the accounts are prepared in
accordance with internationally accepted standards of
presentation and disclosure.
5.        The Consultant will assist EdL in implementing the
recommendations of the Instltutional Development Study Report concerning
finance and accounting and in maintaining accounting records according to
the procedures specified in the manuals. He will discuss and review the
difficulties experienced in practical work and suggest solutions. In
particular, he will guide Lnt he maintenance of project accounting records
in accordance with the Project Accounts Manuai. He will also assist the
finance managers in preparation of monthly income statement, balance sheet
and cash flow statement and the submission of monthly financial reports to
Management.
6.        The Consultant will initiate discussion withi the General
Manager, EdL towards formation of an Internal Audit Department. These
would cover the structuring, scope of work, line of reporting, staffing
and training.
7.        The Consultant and EdL will jointly explore the feasibility of
establishing a working procedure for recovery of electricity arrears from
Government agencies and enterprises.
8.        The Consultant wil  reviow the accounting and billing practiees
of the provincial offices and develop programs for computerizing these
areas of work. The system developed should be simple for use by
provincial staff and should aim at minimum additional hardware at the
provinces. The system should formulate methods by which the computerized
data from the provinces are transferred to the main computer at Vientlane.
Similar procedures should be used for computerizing the other areas such
as stores accounting, maintenance of statistics and management information
systems.
9.        The consultancy services should include adequate provision for
training staff at Vientiane and provincial offices to operate the system
independently.
10.       The Consultant shall crltically review the status and
operational constraints of the computerized billing system presently
operating at Vientiane, and advise EdL for further improvements on the
same to realize the full potential of the system.
11.       The Consultant will sui it periodic reports to EdL on the
progress and status of work. On co pletion of work, the final draft
report will be discussed with EdL and thereafter submitted to EdL and IDA.

of three years. The Consultant's team should comprise of qualLfLed senior

PROVINCIAL GRID INTEGRATION PROJECT
Terms of Reference for Organizatlon and Maagement Study
Background
1.          The Organization and Management Study component of the
Institutional Development Program under the Southern Provinces Electrification
Project (IDA Credit 1826-IA) consists of two phases: (i) analysis of the
existing institutional structure of EdL and proposal for reorganization of the
entity; and (ii) implementation of the proposed reorganization. The initial
phase was launched in July, 1990 and culminated in a reorganizaition proposal
dated January, 1991. The reorganization was approved by EdL management and by
the Ministry of Industry and Handicrafts in September, 1991. Its
implementation began in January, 1992.
Proposed Terms of Reference
2.          In order to implement the recommendations of the reorganizatlon
study, EdL should seek consultancy services to cover the following:
(a) Preparation of a detailed plan to implement the recommendations of
the Organization and Management Study;
(b) Implementation of a Management Information System already
elaborated under Phase I;
(c) Implementation of a human resources management plan through a
newly created Department of Human Resources;
(d) Strengthening the Customer Services Unit;
(e) Introduction and implementation of system planning and tariff
studies; and
(f) Preparation of a system of internal controls to ensure achievement
of EdL objectives.
Objectve
3.          The main objective of the consultancy is to assist EdL to put in
place an organizational structure and procedures that will permit the entlty
to achieve the financial targets and development objectives of its investment
program for 1991-2000. To meet thls objective, it will be necessary for the
Consultant to assist EdL to implement the recommendations set out in the
Organization and Management Study and modifLcations thereto as agreed upon
with EdL management and the Ministry of Industry and Handicrafts. On-the-job

Page 2 of 4
training will be the principal vehicle for implementation of the
reorganization plan.

organization chart. To this end, the Consultant will carry out the activities
and deliver the outputs as described below:

5.          For each of the areas highlighted above, the Consultant will

IAO PDR
PROVINCALL GRIUD INEGRATION PROJECr
T _aing Upgrding Progm
A Program Constituents
1.          The main objectives of the Training Upgrading Program of the
Provincial Grid Integration Project are: (a) to ensure the availability of
trained and motivated managers, technical and non-technical staff to
effectively implement EdL's investment program of the period 1991-2000 and
(b) to develop in-house trained human resources to efficiently operate and
manage the company's assets over the longer term. This Program would continue
efforts to develop the operational skills of EdL's employees, begun under the
Southern Provinces Electrification Project (Credit 1826-LA) through a
combination of in-house trining, short-term fellowships abroad and a
technical cooperation arrangement with an Asian utility. The Program would
extend the provision of equipment and training to the four provincial electric
power subsidiaries, which were integrated into EdL in 1991.
2.          The Trainina Uoarading Proiram would include:
(a)   Renovation of EdL's training center and the provision of equipment
and tools;
(b)   Services of training experts;
(c)   In-house training to strengthen the capabilities of EdLs staff at
all levels; and
(d)   A technical cooperation arrangement between EdL and the National
Electrlcity Board of Malaysia (TNB).
This Annex will describe the Training Upgrading Program only.
3.          EdT TainiLng Uograding Program.  The development of an in-house
capacity to manage EdLs assets would be centered around an upgraded training
center in Vientiane, which would be renovated and equipped with the required
material and tools.  The services of two training consultants--a human
resources expert and an electrLcity transmission and distribution expert would
be required for 25 person-months. Essential tools and equipment will also be
provided to the four provincial subsidiaries. The framework for thls
comprehensive effort to upgrade and develop the theoretical knowledge and
practical, on-the-job skills of technical and non-technical EdL employees is
set out in a document approved by EdL management called "Electricite du Laos:
A Three Year Training Program: 1992-1994," dated September 1991.
4.          Training has been identified for four groups of EdL employees:
(i) managerial staff to ensure the success of the reorganization, upgrade
management skills and identify and address gaps in management techniques;
(ii) technical staff to Lmprove the operation and maintenance of existing



- 74 -
ANNEX 3.7
Page 2 of 4
assets and to effectively operate and maintain newly acquired equipment; and
(iII) non-technical staff to further enhance institutional efficiency (e.g.,
metering control, unpaid bill management) and provide opportunities for
professional growth and (iv) training staff to introduce a long-term EdL
capacity to carry out its own internal training courses and adapt its human
resource programs to changing conditions.
S.          Training topics will address issues in the transmission and
distribution of electricity and human resource development. A new area will
be the development of a customer service unit that can begin to address those
issues on the demand side that concern the understanding of customer behavior
and its impact on long-range power planning for EdL. It is expected that
English language training will be included in the detailed design of the
training program. The design of each training module will take into account
the need to create an adequate incentive environment for all staff to ensure
the success of the program.
6.          Twenty-three training sessions, representing over 3100 person-
weeks of training, will be held in Vientiane. Some training would be in the
form of short-term fellowships abroad of up to six months and some would be
incorporated into a technical cooperation arrangement Tenaga Nasional Berhad
(TNB/Halaysia). A total of 19 programs, representing 612 person-weeks of
training, will be held abroad.
7.          EdL-TNB Technical CogReration Arrangements.  EdL and TNB have
expressed willingness to develop a technical cooperation arrangement program
to further enhance the development of each utility's manpower resources. EdL
would benefit from TNB's broad experience in the growth and development of its
power sector, while TNB would begin to develop the Sultan Ahmad Shah Training
Institute into a regional center to deliver a package of electric utility
training services across the Asia region. The benefits derived by each of the
parties to the technical cooperation arrangement can be expected to strengthen
the prospects for its successful implementation. Monitoring and evaluation of
progress on each aspect of the technical cooperation arrangement will permit
adjustments as required.
8.          It has been proposed that a mix of classroom and hands-on
instruction as well as short-term assignments to a Malaysia utility constitute
tho core of the technical cooperation arrangement. It has been agreed that
the details of the proposed technical cooperation arrangement and exact-mix of
classroom and on-the-job skills development would be defined following an
exchange of visits between TNB and EdL managers. Nonetheless, it is expected
that EdL department managers would be candidates for assignment to a Malaysian
utility, while deputy managers would be candidates for classroom/hanas-on
training at the TNB Training Institute.
9.          Preliminary discussions with TNB and EdL have resulted in the
following broad principles to guide development of the technical cooperation
arrangement relationship:
(a)   The technical cooperation arrangement would be anchored in EdL's
new organizational structure to be implemented in early 1992;



-75 -                              ANNEX 3.7
Page 3 of 4
(b)   The technical cooperation arrangement program would be identified
as a tranche of EdL's overall three-year training program set out
in document dated September 12, 1991 and approved by EdL
management;
(c)   Because of potential language constraints associated with training
in the four provincial subsidiaries, the technical cooperation
arrangement would be limited to EdL headquarters in Vientiane;
(d)   Training of EdL trainers would be a key feature of any technical
cooperation arrangement program;
(e)   A monitoring and evaluation mechanism would be built into the
program; and
(f)   Training would include classroom instruction at the TNB Institute
and attachment to a district utility in peninsular Malaysia wthose
characteristics are closest to those of EdL-Vientiane.
B. Terms of Refereoce for Training Ceter Advisor
10.         B&jgkround.  The expansion of EdL's investment program, together
with the incorporation of the provincial subsidiaries, will require that EdL
become a more efficient utility. A reorganization is in process and a more
formal system of organization and management techniques is under
implementation. These activities should improve operations and heighten the
skills and motivation of EdL staff.
11.         EdL's Training Center plays a key role in upgrading staff skills
and recycling staff for neo functions, as required to meet EdL's expanded
mandate. The Training Advisor will assist in strengthening the Training
Center's capacity to meet this objective.
12.         ProDosed Terms of Reference.  The Training Advisor would provide
technical advice to the Director of the Training Center and would report
directly to the General Manager of EdL. Based on EdLts Three-Year Training
Program (1992-1995) and the recommendations of the Organization and Management
Study (January 1991), the Advisor would, together with the Director of the
Training Center:
(a)   conduct a training needs assessment in the areas of:
(i)   electricity distribution;
(ii) basic skill development;
(iii) organization and management of EdL's operations; and
(iv) managerial skills development;
(b)   based on the needs assessment, develop the objectives for the
Training Program, define the content of the individual courses
which would be taught by Lao trainers, prepare teaching manuals
for each session and establish a system to evaluate the success of
the training courses;



- 76 -
- 76 -                  ~~~~~ANNEX 3.7
Page 4 of 4
(c)   provide training for Lao trainers in pedagogical techniques and
establish a system to evaluate their -,rformance;
(d)   concerning electricity distribution, design training sessions and
teach or ensure that Lao trainers have the technical and
theoretical skills to teach the required courses;
(e)   concerning organization and management, dcsign training sessions
and teach or ensure that Lao trainers have the theoretical skills
to teach the required courses down to the foreman level; and
(f)   provide the required feedback to the General Manager on progress
of the training program and any adjustments required to ensure the
compatibility of the Training Center's curriculum with EdL's
expanding operations.
13.         Imolementation Period.  The period of assignment would be for two
years.
14.         Experienca.  The Training Advisor should have an in-depth
knowledge and experience in the area of electricity distribution; at least
three years experience in developing and implementing utility training
programs; and management and teaching experience. Experience in an Asian
utility and a knowledge of French are highly desirable.

LAO PDR
PROVINCIAL GRID INTEGRATION PROJECT
Technical Cooperation Arrangement Between TNB and EdL
1.          As part of EdL's efforts to strengthen its capacity to operate and
manage its assets, it has been agreed to enter into a technical cooperation
arrangement with Tenaga Nasional Berhad of Malaysia (TNB). The technica.
cooperation arrangement would be identified by TNB and EdL within the context
of a three-year training program (1992-94) recently approved by EdL and which
consists of three main elements:
(i)   in-country training;
(ii)   external training and fellowships; and
(iii)   material and equipment for the renovation of the EdL Training
Center in Vientiane.
2.          Based on the above-mentioned training program, it is expected that
the technical cooperation arrangement with TNB will consist of a package of
classroom training at the TNB Training Institute in Kuala Lumpur, together
with assignments in relevant departments of a district utility in peninsular
Malaysia with characteristics similar to those of Vientiane. Because of
language constraints, it is not expected that the EdL/TNB technical
cooperation arrangement relationship will conccrn the provincial utilities,
although this is subject to review by the technical cooperation arrangement
partners.
3.          It is, furthermore, envisioned that the technical cooperation
arrangement will be designed to correspond to the three year period 1992-94,
according to the following activities:
(i)   1992:  TNB reconnaissance mission to Vientiane, Lao PDR to carry
out a preliminary training needs survey within the framework of
the above-mentioned training program; EdL mission to Kuala Lumpur
to visit TNB's Training Institute and select a suitable district
utility in peninsular Malaysia;
(ii)   1992-1993:  Implementation of agreed upon external training and
utility secondment(s); and
(iii)   1993-1994:  Completion of implementation of technical cooperation
arrangement and monitoring and evaluation of results against
objectives.
4.          The first step in formalizing the proposed technical cooperation
arrangement will consist of a TNB reconnaissance mission to Vientiane to scope
out the training needs and agree with EdL on the content, implementation plan
and costs. In light of EdL's priorities as set out in the aforementioned
training program, the TNB mission would consist of a transmission/distribution



ANNEX 3.8
Page 2 of 2
expert and a management expert. The duration of the mission in Vientiane is
expected to be about ten days.
5.          The mission would:
(i)   together with EdL, identify a package of external training
including classroom-style courses and assignments within a
Malaysian utility. This package would be defined based on EdL's
identified training needs and the comparative advantage of TNB to
deliver technical And non-technical training in English;  The
possibility of including English-language training as part of the
package will also be reviewed and evaluated;
(ii)   based on t1he identified program, TNB will determine the costs
(technical assistance, travel, lodging, per diem, curriculum
costs) of the proposed program, broken down into technical and
non-technical training; nuwbers of trainees; locale (TNB training
institute and/or assignment in a district utility); course
content; and
(iii)   draft a preliminary technical cooperation arrangement protocol
setting out the objectives of the cooperation arrangement and
agreed upon scope and content of the arrangement.
6.          Prior to the mission's departure, TNB should prepare an aide-
memoire, to be agreed upon with EdL, setting out the mission's findings,
including the proposed package of formal training and utility assignment(s);
costs; implementatiorn schedule, monitoring and eNAluation format and any
outstanding issues requiring follow-up action.
7.          An EdL mission will visit MIalaysia approximately one month after
completion of the reconnaissance work to review the final training program and
agree on the selection of a suitable provincial utility.
